     <p>The <b>Chernobyl disaster</b> (also referred to as <b>Chernobyl</b> or <b>the Chernobyl accident</b>) was a <a href="/wiki/Catastrophic_failure" title="Catastrophic failure">catastrophic</a> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents" class="mw-redirect">nuclear accident</a> that occurred on 26 April 1986 at the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Chernobyl Nuclear Power Plant">Chernobyl Nuclear Power Plant</a> in the town of <a href="/wiki/Pripyat,_Ukraine" title="Pripyat, Ukraine" class="mw-redirect">Pripyat</a>, in Ukraine (then officially the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_SSR" title="Ukrainian SSR" class="mw-redirect">Ukrainian SSR</a>), which was under the direct jurisdiction of the central authorities of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. An explosion and fire released large quantities of radioactive particles into the atmosphere, which spread over much of the western USSR and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>.</p> 
     <p>The Chernobyl disaster was the worst <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plant</a> accident in history in terms of cost and casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> It is one of only two classified as a level 7 event (the maximum classification) on the <a href="/wiki/International_Nuclear_Event_Scale" title="International Nuclear Event Scale">International Nuclear Event Scale</a>, the other being the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a> in Japan in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCWorse_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCWorse-2">[2]</a></sup> The battle to contain the contamination and avert a greater catastrophe ultimately involved <a href="/wiki/Liquidator_(Chernobyl)" title="Liquidator (Chernobyl)" class="mw-redirect">over 500,000 workers</a> and cost an estimated 18&nbsp;billion <a href="/wiki/Ruble" title="Ruble">rubles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GorbachevBoC_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GorbachevBoC-3">[3]</a></sup> During the accident itself, <a href="/wiki/Deaths_due_to_the_Chernobyl_disaster" title="Deaths due to the Chernobyl disaster">31 people died</a>, and long-term effects such as cancers are still being investigated.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p></p>

     <p></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Chernobyl_placement.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Chernobyl_placement.svg/220px-Chernobyl_placement.svg.png" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Chernobyl_placement.svg/330px-Chernobyl_placement.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Chernobyl_placement.svg/440px-Chernobyl_placement.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="680" data-file-height="520"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Chernobyl_placement.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Location of Chernobyl nuclear power plant
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG/220px-View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG/330px-View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG/440px-View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2240" data-file-height="1680"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The abandoned city of 
        <a href="/wiki/Pripyat_(city)" title="Pripyat (city)" class="mw-redirect">Pripyat</a> with Chernobyl plant in the distance
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The disaster began during a systems test on 26 April 1986 at reactor number four of the Chernobyl plant, which is near the city of <a href="/wiki/Pripyat" title="Pripyat">Pripyat</a> and in proximity to the administrative border with <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_SSR" title="Belarusian SSR" class="mw-redirect">Belarus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dnieper" title="Dnieper">Dnieper</a> River. There was a sudden and unexpected power surge, and when an emergency shutdown was attempted, an exponentially larger spike in power output occurred, which led to a reactor vessel rupture and a series of steam explosions. These events exposed the <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">moderator</a> of the reactor to air, causing it to ignite.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The resulting fire sent a plume of highly radioactive <a href="/wiki/Fallout" title="Fallout" class="mw-redirect">fallout</a> into the atmosphere and over an extensive geographical area, including Pripyat. The plume drifted over large parts of the western <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and Europe. From 1986 to 2000, 350,400 people were evacuated and resettled from the most severely contaminated areas of Belarus, <a href="/wiki/Russian_SFSR" title="Russian SFSR" class="mw-redirect">Russia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_SSR" title="Ukrainian SSR" class="mw-redirect">Ukraine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> According to official post-Soviet data,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IAEA_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IAEA-8">[8]</a></sup> about 60% of the fallout landed in Belarus.</p> 
     <p>Russia, Ukraine, and Belarus have been burdened with the continuing and substantial <a href="/wiki/Decontamination" title="Decontamination">decontamination</a> and health care costs of the Chernobyl accident. A report by the International Atomic Energy Agency examines the environmental consequences of the accident.<sup id="cite_ref-IAEA_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IAEA-8">[8]</a></sup> Another <a href="/wiki/UN" title="UN" class="mw-redirect">UN</a> agency, <a href="/wiki/UNSCEAR" title="UNSCEAR" class="mw-redirect">UNSCEAR</a>, has estimated a global <a href="/wiki/Collective_dose" title="Collective dose">collective dose</a> of radiation exposure from the accident "equivalent on average to 21 additional days of world exposure to natural <a href="/wiki/Background_radiation" title="Background radiation">background radiation</a>"; individual doses were far higher than the global mean among those most exposed, including 530,000 local recovery workers who averaged an <a href="/wiki/Effective_dose_equivalent" title="Effective dose equivalent" class="mw-redirect">effective dose</a> equivalent to an extra 50 years of typical natural background radiation exposure each.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_2008_D_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_2008_D-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_GA_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_GA-11">[11]</a></sup> Estimates of the number of deaths that will eventually result from the accident vary enormously; disparities reflect both the lack of solid scientific data and the different methodologies used to quantify mortality—whether the discussion is confined to specific geographical areas or extends worldwide, and whether the deaths are immediate, short term, or long term.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Deaths_due_to_the_Chernobyl_disaster" title="Deaths due to the Chernobyl disaster">Thirty-one deaths</a> are directly attributed to the accident, all among the reactor staff and emergency workers.<sup id="cite_ref-Hallenbeck_1994_15_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hallenbeck_1994_15-12">[12]</a></sup> An UNSCEAR report places the total confirmed deaths from radiation at 64 as of 2008. The <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_Forum" title="Chernobyl Forum">Chernobyl Forum</a> predicts the eventual death toll could reach 4,000 among those exposed to the highest levels of radiation (200,000 emergency workers, 116,000 evacuees and 270,000 residents of the most contaminated areas); this figure is a total <a href="/wiki/Causal" title="Causal" class="mw-redirect">causal</a> death toll prediction, combining the deaths of approximately 50 emergency workers who died soon after the accident from <a href="/wiki/Acute_radiation_syndrome" title="Acute radiation syndrome">acute radiation syndrome</a>, nine children who have died of <a href="/wiki/Thyroid_cancer" title="Thyroid cancer">thyroid cancer</a> and a future predicted total of 3940 deaths from radiation-induced cancer and leukemia.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In a <a href="/wiki/Peer-reviewed" title="Peer-reviewed" class="mw-redirect">peer-reviewed</a> publication in the <i><a href="/wiki/International_Journal_of_Cancer" title="International Journal of Cancer">International Journal of Cancer</a></i> in 2006, the authors (following a different conclusion methodology to the Chernobyl Forum study, which arrived at the total predicted death toll of 4,000 after <a href="/wiki/List_of_cancer_mortality_rates_in_the_United_States" title="List of cancer mortality rates in the United States">cancer survival rates</a> were factored in) stated, without entering into a discussion on deaths, that in terms of total excess cancers attributed to the accident:<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p> 
     <blockquote class="templatequote"> 
      <p>The risk projections suggest that by now Chernobyl may have caused about 1000 cases of thyroid cancer and 4000 cases of other cancers in Europe, representing about 0.01% of all incident cancers since the accident. Models predict that by 2065 about 16,000 cases of thyroid cancer and 25,000 cases of other cancers may be expected due to radiation from the accident, whereas several hundred million cancer cases are expected from other causes.</p> 
     </blockquote> 
     <p>Also based upon extrapolations from the <a href="/wiki/Linear_no-threshold_model" title="Linear no-threshold model">linear no-threshold model</a> of radiation induced damage, down to zero, the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_Concerned_Scientists" title="Union of Concerned Scientists">Union of Concerned Scientists</a> estimates that, among the hundreds of millions of people living in broader geographical areas, there will be 50,000 excess cancer cases resulting in 25,000 excess cancer deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>For this broader group, the 2006 <a href="/wiki/TORCH_report" title="TORCH report">TORCH report</a>, commissioned by the <a href="/wiki/European_Greens" title="European Greens" class="mw-redirect">European Greens</a> political party, predicts 30,000 to 60,000 excess cancer deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-torch_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-torch-16">[16]</a></sup> In terms of non-<a href="/wiki/Scientific" title="Scientific" class="mw-redirect">scientific</a> publications, two affiliated with the anti-nuclear advocacy group <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a>, have been released, one of which reports the figure at 200,000 or more.<sup id="cite_ref-greenpeace2006_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenpeace2006-17">[17]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The Russian founder of that region's chapter of Greenpeace also authored a book titled <i><a href="/wiki/Chernobyl:_Consequences_of_the_Catastrophe_for_People_and_the_Environment" title="Chernobyl: Consequences of the Catastrophe for People and the Environment">Chernobyl: Consequences of the Catastrophe for People and the Environment</a></i>, which concludes that among the billions of people worldwide who were exposed to radioactive contamination from the disaster, nearly a million premature cancer deaths occurred between 1986 and 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-Alexey2009_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alexey2009-18">[18]</a></sup> The book, however, has failed the <a href="/wiki/Peer_review" title="Peer review">peer review</a> process.<sup id="cite_ref-MonbiotEvidenceMeltdown_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MonbiotEvidenceMeltdown-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Of the five reviews published in the academic press, four considered the book severely flawed and contradictory, and one praised it while noting some shortcomings. The review by M. I. Balonov published by the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Academy_of_Sciences" title="New York Academy of Sciences">New York Academy of Sciences</a> concludes that the report is of negative value because it has very little <a href="/wiki/Scientific" title="Scientific" class="mw-redirect">scientific</a> merit while being highly misleading to the lay reader. It characterized the estimate of nearly a million deaths as more in the realm of <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> than science.<sup id="cite_ref-balonov_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-balonov-21">[21]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The accident raised concerns about nuclear power worldwide and slowed or <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_phase-out" title="Nuclear power phase-out">reversed</a> the expansion of nuclear power stations.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The accident also raised concerns about the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety" title="Nuclear safety" class="mw-redirect">safety</a> of the Soviet nuclear power industry, slowing its expansion for a number of years and forcing the Soviet government to become less secretive about its procedures.<sup id="cite_ref-Kagarlitsky_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kagarlitsky-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kagarlitsky_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kagarlitsky-24">[notes 1]</a></sup> The government coverup of the Chernobyl disaster was a "catalyst" for <a href="/wiki/Glasnost" title="Glasnost">glasnost</a>, which "paved the way for reforms leading to the Soviet collapse".<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accident">Accident</span></h2> 
     <p>On 26 April 1986, at 01:23 (<a href="/wiki/Moscow_Time" title="Moscow Time">UTC+3</a>), reactor four suffered a catastrophic power increase, leading to explosions in its core. This dispersed large quantities of radioactive fuel and core materials into the atmosphere<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:73</sup> and ignited the combustible <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_graphite" title="Nuclear graphite">graphite</a> moderator. The burning graphite moderator increased the emission of <a href="/wiki/Hot_particle" title="Hot particle">radioactive particles</a>, carried by the smoke, as the reactor had not been encased by any kind of hard <a href="/wiki/Containment_building" title="Containment building">containment vessel</a>. The accident occurred during an experiment scheduled to test a potential safety <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety_systems#Emergency_core_cooling_system" title="Nuclear safety systems" class="mw-redirect">emergency core cooling</a> feature, which took place during a normal shutdown procedure.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steam_turbine_tests">Steam turbine tests</span></h3> 
     <p>In steady state operation, a significant fraction (about 7%) of the power from a nuclear reactor comes not from fission but from the <a href="/wiki/Decay_heat" title="Decay heat">decay heat</a> of its accumulated fission products. This heat continues for some time after the chain reaction is stopped (e.g., following an emergency <a href="/wiki/SCRAM" title="SCRAM" class="mw-redirect">SCRAM</a>) and usually requires active cooling to avoid <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_meltdown" title="Nuclear meltdown">core damage</a>. <a href="/wiki/RBMK" title="RBMK">RBMK</a> reactors, like those at Chernobyl, use water as a coolant.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> Reactor 4 at Chernobyl consisted of about 1,600 individual fuel channels; each required a coolant flow of 28 metric tons (28,000 liters or 7,400 U.S. gallons) per hour.<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Since cooling pumps require electricity to cool a reactor after a SCRAM, in the event of a power grid failure, Chernobyl's reactors had three backup <a href="/wiki/Diesel_generator" title="Diesel generator">diesel generators</a>; these could start up in 15 seconds, but took 60–75 seconds<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:15</sup> to attain full speed and reach the 5.5‑<a href="/wiki/Megawatt" title="Megawatt" class="mw-redirect">megawatt</a> (MW) output required to run one main pump.<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:30</sup></p> 
     <p>To solve this one-minute gap, considered an unacceptable safety risk, it had been theorised that <a href="/wiki/Rotational_energy" title="Rotational energy">rotational energy</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a> (as it wound down under residual steam pressure) could be used to generate the required electrical power. Analysis indicated that this residual momentum and steam pressure might be sufficient to run the coolant pumps for 45 seconds,<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:16</sup> bridging the gap between an external power failure and the full availability of the emergency generators.<sup id="cite_ref-NV_Karpan:_312.E2.80.9313_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NV_Karpan:_312.E2.80.9313-29">[28]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>This capability still needed to be confirmed experimentally, and previous tests had ended unsuccessfully. An initial test carried out in 1982 showed that the <a href="/wiki/Excitation_(magnetic)" title="Excitation (magnetic)">excitation</a> voltage of the turbine-generator was insufficient; it did not maintain the desired <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> after the turbine trip. The system was modified, and the test was repeated in 1984 but again proved unsuccessful. In 1985, the tests were attempted a third time but also yielded negative results. The test procedure was to be repeated again in 1986, and it was scheduled to take place during the maintenance shutdown of Reactor Four.<sup id="cite_ref-NV_Karpan:_312.E2.80.9313_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NV_Karpan:_312.E2.80.9313-29">[28]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The test focused on the switching sequences of the electrical supplies for the reactor. The test procedure was to begin with an automatic emergency shutdown. No detrimental effect on the safety of the reactor was anticipated, so the test program was not formally coordinated with either the chief designer of the reactor (NIKIET) or the scientific manager. Instead, it was approved only by the director of the plant (and even this approval was not consistent with established procedures).<sup id="cite_ref-insag7_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-insag7-30">[29]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>According to the test parameters, the thermal output of the reactor should have been <i>no lower</i> than 700&nbsp;MW at the start of the experiment. If test conditions had been as planned, the procedure would almost certainly have been carried out safely; the eventual disaster resulted from attempts to boost the reactor output once the experiment had been started, which was inconsistent with approved procedure.<sup id="cite_ref-insag7_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-insag7-30">[29]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The Chernobyl power plant had been in operation for two years without the capability to ride through the first 60–75 seconds of a total loss of electric power, and thus lacked an important safety feature. The station managers presumably wished to correct this at the first opportunity, which may explain why they continued the test even when serious problems arose, and why the requisite approval for the test had not been sought from the Soviet nuclear oversight regulator (even though there was a representative at the complex of 4 reactors).<sup id="cite_ref-Medvedev_Z_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medvedev_Z-31">[notes 2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:18–20</sup></p> 
     <p>The experimental procedure was intended to run as follows:</p> 
     <ol> 
      <li>The reactor was to be running at a low power level, between 700 MW and 800 MW.</li> 
      <li>The steam-turbine generator was to be run up to full speed.</li> 
      <li>When these conditions were achieved, the steam supply for the turbine generator was to be closed off.</li> 
      <li>Turbine generator performance was to be recorded to determine whether it could provide the bridging power for coolant pumps until the emergency diesel generators were sequenced to start and provide power to the cooling pumps automatically.</li> 
      <li>After the emergency generators reached normal operating speed and voltage, the turbine generator would be allowed to continue to freewheel down.</li> 
     </ol> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conditions_before_the_accident">Conditions before the accident</span></h3> 
     <p>The conditions to run the test were established before the day shift of 25 April 1986. The day shift workers had been instructed in advance and were familiar with the established procedures. A special team of <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineer" title="Electrical engineer" class="mw-redirect">electrical engineers</a> was present to test the new voltage regulating system.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> As planned, a gradual reduction in the output of the power unit was begun at 01:06 on 25 April, and the power level had reached 50% of its nominal 3200&nbsp;MW thermal level by the beginning of the day shift.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:RBMK_en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/RBMK_en.svg/400px-RBMK_en.svg.png" width="400" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/RBMK_en.svg/600px-RBMK_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/RBMK_en.svg/800px-RBMK_en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="950" data-file-height="510"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:RBMK_en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> A schematic diagram of the reactor
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>At this point, another regional power station unexpectedly went offline, and the <a href="/wiki/Kiev" title="Kiev">Kiev</a> <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">electrical grid</a> controller requested that the further reduction of Chernobyl's output be postponed, as power was needed to satisfy the peak evening demand. The Chernobyl plant director agreed, and postponed the test. Despite this postponement, preparations for the test not affecting the reactor's power were carried out, including the disabling of the emergency core cooling system or ECCS, a passive/active system of core cooling intended to provide water to the core in a loss-of-coolant accident. Given the other events that unfolded, the system would have been of limited use, but its disabling as a "routine" step of the test is an illustration of the inherent lack of attention to safety for this test.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD02-Ch1_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD02-Ch1-33">[31]</a></sup> In addition, had the reactor been shut down for the day as planned, it is possible that more preparation would have been taken in advance of the test.</p> 
     <p>At 23:04, the Kiev grid controller allowed the reactor shutdown to resume. This delay had some serious consequences: the day shift had long since departed, the evening shift was also preparing to leave, and the night shift would not take over until midnight, well into the job. According to plan, the test should have been finished during the day shift, and the night shift would only have had to maintain decay heat cooling systems in an otherwise shut-down plant.<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:36–38</sup></p> 
     <p>The night shift had very limited time to prepare for and carry out the experiment. A further rapid reduction in the power level from 50% was executed during the shift change-over. <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Akimov" title="Alexander Akimov">Alexander Akimov</a> was chief of the night shift, and Leonid Toptunov was the operator responsible for the reactor's operational regimen, including the movement of the <a href="/wiki/Control_rod" title="Control rod">control rods</a>. Toptunov was a young engineer who had worked independently as a senior engineer for approximately three months.<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:36–38</sup></p> 
     <p>The test plan called for a gradual reduction in power output from reactor 4 to a thermal level of 700–1000&nbsp;MW.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup> An output of 700&nbsp;MW was reached at 00:05 on 26 April. However, due to the natural production of <a href="/wiki/Xenon-135" title="Xenon-135">xenon-135</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_absorber" title="Neutron absorber" class="mw-redirect">neutron absorber</a>, from the decay of the fission product <a href="/wiki/Iodine-135" title="Iodine-135" class="mw-redirect">iodine-135</a>, core power continued to decrease without further operator action—a process known as <a href="/wiki/Iodine_pit" title="Iodine pit">reactor poisoning</a>. (In steady state operation, xenon-135 is "burned off" as it is created from iodine-135 by absorbing neutrons from the ongoing chain reaction and becoming stable xenon-136. But when reactor power is lowered, previously produced iodine-135 decays into xenon-135 faster than the reduced neutron flux can destroy it.) As the reactor power output dropped further, to approximately 500&nbsp;MW, Toptunov mistakenly inserted the control rods too far—the exact circumstances leading to this are unknown because Akimov and Toptunov died in the hospital on May 10 and 14, respectively. This combination of factors rendered the reactor in an unintended near-<a href="/wiki/Shutdown_(nuclear_reactor)" title="Shutdown (nuclear reactor)">shutdown</a> state, with a power output of 30&nbsp;MW thermal or less.</p> 
     <p>The reactor was now producing 5&nbsp;percent of the minimum initial power level established as safe for the test.<sup id="cite_ref-insag7_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-insag7-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:73</sup> Control-room personnel decided to restore power by disabling the automatic system governing the control rods and manually extracting the majority of the reactor control rods to their upper limits.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> Several minutes elapsed between their extraction and the point that the power output began to increase and subsequently stabilize at 160–200&nbsp;MW (thermal), a much smaller value than the planned 700&nbsp;MW. The rapid reduction in the power during the initial shutdown, and the subsequent operation at a level of less than 200&nbsp;MW led to increased <a href="/wiki/Reactor_poisoning" title="Reactor poisoning" class="mw-redirect">poisoning</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Reactor_core" title="Reactor core" class="mw-redirect">reactor core</a> by the accumulation of xenon-135.<sup id="cite_ref-nf_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nf-36">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> This restricted any further rise of reactor power, and made it necessary to extract additional control rods from the reactor core in order to counteract the poisoning.</p> 
     <p>The operation of the reactor at the low power level and high poisoning level was accompanied by unstable core temperature and coolant flow, and possibly by instability of neutron flux, which triggered alarms. The control room received repeated emergency signals regarding the levels in the steam/water separator drums, and large excursions or variations in the flow rate of feed water, as well as from <a href="/wiki/Relief_valve" title="Relief valve">relief valves</a> opened to relieve excess steam into a <a href="/wiki/Condenser_(heat_transfer)" title="Condenser (heat transfer)">turbine condenser</a>, and from the neutron power controller. In the period between 00:35 and 00:45, emergency alarm signals concerning <a href="/wiki/Thermal-hydraulic" title="Thermal-hydraulic" class="mw-redirect">thermal-hydraulic</a> parameters were ignored, apparently to preserve the reactor power level.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>When the power level of 200&nbsp;MW was eventually achieved, preparation for the experiment continued. As part of the test plan, extra water pumps were activated at 01:05 on 26 April, increasing the water flow. The increased coolant flow rate through the reactor produced an increase in the inlet coolant temperature of the reactor core (the coolant no longer having sufficient time to release its heat in the turbine and cooling towers), which now more closely approached the <a href="/wiki/Nucleate_boiling" title="Nucleate boiling">nucleate boiling</a> temperature of water, reducing the <a href="/wiki/Factor_of_safety" title="Factor of safety">safety margin</a>.</p> 
     <p>The flow exceeded the allowed limit at 01:19, triggering an alarm of low steam pressure in the steam separators. At the same time, the extra water flow lowered the overall core temperature and reduced the existing <a href="/wiki/Void_coefficient" title="Void coefficient">steam voids</a> in the core and the steam separators.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> Since water weakly absorbs neutrons (and the higher density of liquid water makes it a better absorber than steam), turning on additional pumps decreased the reactor power further still. The crew responded by turning off two of the circulation pumps to reduce feedwater flow, in an effort to increase steam pressure, and also to remove more manual control rods to maintain power.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD02-Ch1_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD02-Ch1-33">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>All these actions led to an extremely unstable reactor configuration. Nearly all of the control rods were removed manually, including all but 18 of the "fail-safe" manually operated rods of the minimal 28 which were intended to remain fully inserted to control the reactor even in the event of a loss of coolant, out of a total 211 control rods.<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevG_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevG-41">[39]</a></sup> While the emergency SCRAM system that would insert all control rods to shut down the reactor could still be activated manually (through the "AZ-5" button), the automated system that could do the same had been disabled to maintain the power level, and many other automated and even passive safety features of the reactor had been bypassed. Further, the reactor coolant pumping had been reduced, which had limited margin so any power excursion would produce boiling, thereby reducing <a href="/wiki/Neutron_absorption" title="Neutron absorption" class="mw-redirect">neutron absorption</a> by the water. The reactor was in an unstable configuration that was clearly outside the safe operating envelope established by the designers. If anything pushed it into supercriticality, it was unable to recover automatically.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Experiment_and_explosion">Experiment and explosion</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:239px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Chernobyl_burning-aerial_view_of_core.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f6/Chernobyl_burning-aerial_view_of_core.jpg/237px-Chernobyl_burning-aerial_view_of_core.jpg" width="237" height="320" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f6/Chernobyl_burning-aerial_view_of_core.jpg/356px-Chernobyl_burning-aerial_view_of_core.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/f6/Chernobyl_burning-aerial_view_of_core.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="540"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Chernobyl_burning-aerial_view_of_core.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Aerial view of the damaged core on 3 May 1986. Roof of the turbine hall is damaged (image center). Roof of the adjacent reactor 3 (image lower left) shows minor fire damage.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>At 1:23:04&nbsp;a.m., the experiment began. Four of the Main Circulating Pumps (MCP) were active; of the eight total, six are normally active during regular operation. The steam to the turbines was shut off, beginning a run-down of the turbine generator. The diesel generators started and sequentially picked up loads; the generators were to have completely picked up the MCPs' power needs by 01:23:43. In the interim, the power for the MCPs was to be supplied by the turbine generator as it coasted down. As the <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> of the turbine generator decreased, however, so did the power it produced for the pumps. The water flow rate decreased, leading to increased formation of steam voids (bubbles) in the core.</p> 
     <p>Because of the positive void coefficient of the RBMK reactor at low reactor power levels, it was now primed to embark on a <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a> loop, in which the formation of steam voids reduced the ability of the liquid water <a href="/wiki/Coolant" title="Coolant">coolant</a> to absorb neutrons, which in turn increased the reactor's power output. This caused yet more water to flash into steam, giving yet a further power increase. During almost the entire period of the experiment the automatic control system successfully counteracted this positive feedback, continuously inserting <a href="/wiki/Control_rod" title="Control rod">control rods</a> into the reactor core to limit the power rise. However, this system had control of only 12 rods, and nearly all others had been manually retracted.</p> 
     <p>At 1:23:40, as recorded by the <a href="/wiki/SKALA" title="SKALA">SKALA</a> centralized control system, an emergency shutdown of the reactor, which inadvertently triggered the explosion, was initiated. The SCRAM was started when the EPS-5 button (also known as the AZ-5 button) of the reactor emergency protection system was pressed: this engaged the drive mechanism on all control rods to fully insert them, including the manual control rods that had been incautiously withdrawn earlier. The reason why the EPS-5 button was pressed is not known, whether it was done as an emergency measure in response to rising temperatures, or simply as a routine method of shutting down the reactor upon completion of the experiment.</p> 
     <p>There is a view that the SCRAM may have been ordered as a response to the unexpected rapid power increase, although there is no recorded data conclusively proving this. Some have suggested that the button was not pressed, and instead the signal was automatically produced by the emergency protection system; however, the SKALA clearly registered a manual SCRAM signal. In spite of this, the question as to when or even whether the EPS-5 button was pressed has been the subject of debate. There are assertions that the pressure was caused by the rapid power acceleration at the start, and allegations that the button was not pressed until the reactor began to self-destruct but others assert that it happened earlier and in calm conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:578</sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>After the EPS-5 button was pressed, the insertion of control rods into the reactor core began. The control rod insertion mechanism moved the rods at 0.4&nbsp;m/s, so that the rods took 18 to 20&nbsp;seconds to travel the full height of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor_core" title="Nuclear reactor core">core</a>, about 7 meters. A bigger problem was a flawed graphite-tip control rod design, which initially displaced neutron-absorbing coolant with moderating graphite before introducing replacement neutron-absorbing boron material to slow the reaction. As a result, the SCRAM actually increased the reaction rate in the upper half of the core as the tips displaced water. This behavior was known after a shutdown of another RBMK reactor induced an initial power spike, but as the SCRAM of that reactor was successful, the information was not widely disseminated.</p> 
     <p>A few seconds after the start of the SCRAM, the graphite rod tips entered the fuel pile. A massive power spike occurred, and the core overheated, causing some of the <a href="/wiki/Fuel_rod" title="Fuel rod" class="mw-redirect">fuel rods</a> to fracture, blocking the control rod columns and jamming the control rods at one-third insertion, with the graphite tips in the middle of the core. Within three seconds the reactor output rose above 530&nbsp;MW.<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:31</sup></p> 
     <p>The subsequent course of events was not registered by instruments; it is known only as a result of mathematical simulation. Apparently, the power spike caused an increase in fuel temperature and massive steam buildup, leading to a rapid increase in steam pressure. This caused the fuel cladding to fail, releasing the fuel elements into the coolant, and rupturing the channels in which these elements were located.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Then, according to some estimations, the reactor jumped to around 30,000&nbsp;MW thermal, ten times the normal operational output. The last reading on the control panel was 33,000&nbsp;MW. It was not possible to reconstruct the precise sequence of the processes that led to the destruction of the reactor and the power unit building, but a <a href="/wiki/Steam_explosion" title="Steam explosion">steam explosion</a>, like the explosion of a <a href="/wiki/Steam_boiler" title="Steam boiler" class="mw-redirect">steam boiler</a> from excess vapor pressure, appears to have been the next event. There is a general understanding that it was explosive steam pressure from the damaged fuel channels escaping into the reactor's exterior cooling structure that caused the detonation that destroyed the reactor casing, tearing off and blasting the 2000-ton upper plate, to which the entire reactor assembly is fastened, through the roof of the reactor building. This is believed to be the first explosion that many heard.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:366</sup> This explosion ruptured further fuel channels, as well as severing most of the coolant lines feeding the reactor chamber, and as a result the remaining coolant flashed to steam and escaped the reactor core. The total water loss in combination with a high positive void coefficient further increased the reactor's thermal power.</p> 
     <p>A second, more powerful explosion occurred about two or three seconds after the first; this explosion dispersed the damaged core and effectively terminated the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">nuclear chain reaction</a>. However, this explosion also compromised more of the reactor containment vessel and ejected superheated lumps of graphite moderator. The ejected graphite and the demolished channels still in the remains of the reactor vessel caught fire on exposure to air, greatly contributing to the spread of <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_fallout" title="Radioactive fallout" class="mw-redirect">radioactive fallout</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_contamination" title="Radioactive contamination">contamination</a> of outlying areas.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD02-Ch1_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD02-Ch1-33">[31]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>According to observers outside Unit 4, burning lumps of material and sparks shot into the air above the reactor. Some of them fell onto the roof of the machine hall and started a fire. About 25 percent of the red-hot graphite blocks and overheated material from the fuel channels was ejected. Parts of the graphite blocks and fuel channels were out of the reactor building. As a result of the damage to the building an airflow through the core was established by the high temperature of the core. The air ignited the hot graphite and started a graphite fire.<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:32</sup></p> 
     <p>There were initially several hypotheses about the nature of the second explosion. One view was that the second explosion was caused by <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, which had been produced either by the overheated steam-<a href="/wiki/Zircaloy" title="Zircaloy" class="mw-redirect">zirconium</a> reaction or by the <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">reaction of red-hot graphite with steam</a> that produced hydrogen and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>. Another hypothesis was that the second explosion was a thermal explosion of the reactor as a result of the uncontrollable escape of <a href="/wiki/Fast_neutron" title="Fast neutron" class="mw-redirect">fast neutrons</a> caused by the complete water loss in the reactor core.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup> A third hypothesis was that the explosion was a second steam explosion. According to this version, the first explosion was a more minor steam explosion in the circulating loop, causing a loss of coolant flow and pressure, that in turn caused the water still in the core to flash to steam. This second explosion then did the majority of the damage to the reactor and containment building.</p> 
     <p>However, the sheer force of the second explosion, and the ratio of <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_xenon" title="Isotopes of xenon">xenon radioisotopes</a> released during the event, indicate that the second explosion could have been a nuclear power transient; the result of the melting core material, in the absence of its cladding, water coolant and moderator, undergoing <a href="/wiki/Criticality_accident" title="Criticality accident">runaway prompt criticality</a> similar to the explosion of a <a href="/wiki/Fizzle_(nuclear_test)" title="Fizzle (nuclear test)">fizzled nuclear weapon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pakhomov2009_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pakhomov2009-47">[45]</a></sup> This nuclear excursion released 40&nbsp;billion <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joules</a> of energy, the equivalent of about ten tons of <a href="/wiki/TNT_equivalent" title="TNT equivalent">TNT</a>. The analysis indicates that the nuclear excursion was limited to a small portion of the core.<sup id="cite_ref-Pakhomov2009_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pakhomov2009-47">[45]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Contrary to safety regulations, <a href="/wiki/Bitumen" title="Bitumen" class="mw-redirect">bitumen</a>, a combustible material, had been used in the construction of the roof of the reactor building and the turbine hall. Ejected material ignited at least five fires on the roof of the adjacent reactor 3, which was still operating. It was imperative to put those fires out and protect the cooling systems of reactor 3.<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:42</sup> Inside reactor 3, the chief of the night shift, Yuri Bagdasarov, wanted to shut down the reactor immediately, but chief engineer Nikolai Fomin would not allow this. The operators were given <a href="/wiki/Respirator" title="Respirator">respirators</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_iodide" title="Potassium iodide">potassium iodide</a> tablets and told to continue working. At 05:00, however, Bagdasarov made his own decision to shut down the reactor, leaving only those operators there who had to work the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety_systems" title="Nuclear safety systems" class="mw-redirect">emergency cooling systems</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:44</sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation_levels">Radiation levels</span></h4>

     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Individual_involvement">Individual involvement</span></h4> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Individual_involvement_in_the_Chernobyl_disaster" title="Individual involvement in the Chernobyl disaster">Individual involvement in the Chernobyl disaster</a>
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immediate_crisis_management">Immediate crisis management</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Levels_of_radioactivity_in_the_lava_under_the_Chernobyl_number_four_reactor_1986.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Levels_of_radioactivity_in_the_lava_under_the_Chernobyl_number_four_reactor_1986.svg/350px-Levels_of_radioactivity_in_the_lava_under_the_Chernobyl_number_four_reactor_1986.svg.png" width="350" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Levels_of_radioactivity_in_the_lava_under_the_Chernobyl_number_four_reactor_1986.svg/525px-Levels_of_radioactivity_in_the_lava_under_the_Chernobyl_number_four_reactor_1986.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Levels_of_radioactivity_in_the_lava_under_the_Chernobyl_number_four_reactor_1986.svg/700px-Levels_of_radioactivity_in_the_lava_under_the_Chernobyl_number_four_reactor_1986.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1692" data-file-height="750"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Levels_of_radioactivity_in_the_lava_under_the_Chernobyl_number_four_reactor_1986.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Extremely high levels of radioactivity in the lava under the Chernobyl number four reactor in 1986
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation_levels_2">Radiation levels</span></h4> 
     <p>The radiation levels in the worst-hit areas of the reactor building have been estimated to be 5.6&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Roentgen_(unit)" title="Roentgen (unit)">roentgens</a> per second (R/s), equivalent to more than 20,000&nbsp;roentgens per hour. A lethal dose is around 500&nbsp;roentgens (~5&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Gray_(unit)" title="Gray (unit)">Gy</a>) over 5&nbsp;hours, so in some areas, unprotected workers received fatal doses in less than a minute. However, a <a href="/wiki/Dosimeter" title="Dosimeter">dosimeter</a> capable of measuring up to 1000&nbsp;R/s was buried in the rubble of a collapsed part of the building, and another one failed when turned on. All remaining dosimeters had limits of 0.001&nbsp;R/s and therefore read "off scale". Thus, the reactor crew could ascertain only that the radiation levels were somewhere above 0.001&nbsp;R/s (3.6&nbsp;R/h), while the true levels were much higher in some areas.<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:42–50</sup></p> 
     <p>Because of the inaccurate low readings, the reactor crew chief Alexander Akimov assumed that the reactor was intact. The evidence of pieces of graphite and reactor fuel lying around the building was ignored, and the readings of another dosimeter brought in by 04:30 were dismissed under the assumption that the new dosimeter must have been defective.<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:42–50</sup> Akimov stayed with his crew in the reactor building until morning, sending members of his crew to try to pump water into the reactor. None of them wore any protective gear. Most, including Akimov, died from radiation exposure within three weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevG_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevG-41">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:247–48</sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fire_containment">Fire containment</span></h4> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Leonid_Telyatnikov_(1951-2004)_decorated_in_UK.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0b/Leonid_Telyatnikov_%281951-2004%29_decorated_in_UK.jpg/250px-Leonid_Telyatnikov_%281951-2004%29_decorated_in_UK.jpg" width="250" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0b/Leonid_Telyatnikov_%281951-2004%29_decorated_in_UK.jpg/375px-Leonid_Telyatnikov_%281951-2004%29_decorated_in_UK.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/0b/Leonid_Telyatnikov_%281951-2004%29_decorated_in_UK.jpg 2x" data-file-width="461" data-file-height="386"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Leonid_Telyatnikov_(1951-2004)_decorated_in_UK.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Firefighter 
        <a href="/wiki/Leonid_Telyatnikov" title="Leonid Telyatnikov">Leonid Telyatnikov</a>, being decorated for bravery
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Shortly after the accident, firefighters arrived to try to extinguish the fires. First on the scene was a Chernobyl Power Station firefighter brigade under the command of Lieutenant <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Pravik" title="Vladimir Pravik">Volodymyr Pravik</a>, who died on 9 May 1986 of <a href="/wiki/Radiation_poisoning" title="Radiation poisoning" class="mw-redirect">acute radiation sickness</a>. They were not told how dangerously radioactive the smoke and the debris were, and may not even have known that the accident was anything more than a regular <a href="/wiki/Electrical_fire" title="Electrical fire" class="mw-redirect">electrical fire</a>: "We didn't know it was the reactor. No one had told us."<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Grigorii Khmel, the driver of one of the <a href="/wiki/Fire_engine" title="Fire engine">fire engines</a>, later described what happened:</p> 
     <blockquote class="templatequote"> 
      <p>We arrived there at 10 or 15 minutes to two in the morning.... We saw graphite scattered about. Misha asked: "Is that graphite?" I kicked it away. But one of the fighters on the other truck picked it up. "It's hot," he said. The pieces of graphite were of different sizes, some big, some small, enough to pick them up...</p> 
      <p>We didn't know much about radiation. Even those who worked there had no idea. There was no water left in the trucks. Misha filled a <a href="/wiki/Cistern" title="Cistern">cistern</a> and we aimed the water at the top. Then those boys who died went up to the roof – Vashchik, Kolya and others, and Volodya Pravik.... They went up the ladder ... and I never saw them again.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:54</sup></p> 
     </blockquote> 
     <p>Anatoli Zakharov, a fireman stationed in Chernobyl since 1980, offers a different description in 2008:</p> 
     <blockquote class="templatequote"> 
      <p>I remember joking to the others, "There must be an incredible amount of radiation here. We'll be lucky if we're all still alive in the morning."<sup id="cite_ref-nuclruss_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nuclruss-52">[50]</a></sup></p> 
     </blockquote> 
     <p>He also said:</p> 
     <blockquote class="templatequote"> 
      <p>Of course we knew! If we'd followed regulations, we would never have gone near the reactor. But it was a moral obligation – our duty. We were like <a href="/wiki/Kamikaze" title="Kamikaze">kamikaze</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nuclruss_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nuclruss-52">[50]</a></sup></p> 
     </blockquote> 
     <p>The immediate priority was to extinguish fires on the roof of the station and the area around the building containing Reactor No.&nbsp;4 to protect No.&nbsp;3 and keep its core cooling systems intact. The fires were extinguished by 5:00, but many firefighters received high doses of radiation. The fire inside reactor 4 continued to burn until 10 May 1986; it is possible that well over half of the graphite burned out.<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:73</sup></p> 
     <p>The fire was extinguished by a combined effort of helicopters dropping over 5000&nbsp;metric tons of sand, lead, clay, and <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron-absorbing</a> <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> onto the burning reactor and injection of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">liquid nitrogen</a>. The Ukrainian filmmaker <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vladimir_Shevchenko&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Vladimir Shevchenko (page does not exist)">Vladimir Shevchenko</a> captured film footage of an <a href="/wiki/Mil_Mi-8" title="Mil Mi-8">Mi-8</a> helicopter as its main rotor collided with a nearby <a href="/wiki/Construction_crane" title="Construction crane" class="mw-redirect">construction crane</a> cable, causing the helicopter to fall near the damaged reactor building and killing its four-man crew.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> It is now known that virtually none of the neutron absorbers reached the core.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCContaining_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCContaining-54">[52]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>From eyewitness accounts of the firefighters involved before they died (as reported on the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Canadian Broadcasting Corporation">CBC</a> television series <a href="/wiki/Witness_(TV_series)" title="Witness (TV series)"><i>Witness</i></a>), one described his experience of the radiation as "tasting like metal", and feeling a sensation similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Paresthesia" title="Paresthesia">pins and needles</a> all over his face. (This is similar to the description given by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Slotin" title="Louis Slotin">Louis Slotin</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a> physicist who died days after a fatal radiation overdose from a <a href="/wiki/Demon_core" title="Demon core">criticality accident</a>.)<sup id="cite_ref-zeilig22_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zeilig22-55">[53]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The explosion and fire threw hot particles of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">nuclear fuel</a> and also far more dangerous <a href="/wiki/Fission_product" title="Fission product" class="mw-redirect">fission products</a>, radioactive isotopes such as <a href="/wiki/Caesium-137" title="Caesium-137">caesium-137</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iodine-131" title="Iodine-131">iodine-131</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strontium-90" title="Strontium-90">strontium-90</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Radionuclides" title="Radionuclides" class="mw-redirect">radionuclides</a>, into the air: the residents of the surrounding area observed the radioactive cloud on the night of the explosion.</p> 
     <p>Equipment assembled included remote-controlled bulldozers and robot-carts that could detect radioactivity and carry hot debris. Valery Legasov (first deputy director of the Kurchatov Institute of Atomic Energy in Moscow) said, in 1987: "But we learned that robots are not the great remedy for everything. Where there was very high radiation, the robot ceased to be a robot—the electronics quit working."<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-56">[54]</a></sup></p> 
     <h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline">Timeline</span></h5> 
     <ul> 
      <li>1:26:03 – fire alarm activated</li> 
      <li>1:28 – arrival of local firefighters, Pravik's guard</li> 
      <li>1:35 – arrival of firefighters from Pripyat, Kibenok's guard</li> 
      <li>1:40 – arrival of Telyatnikov</li> 
      <li>2:10 – turbine hall roof fire extinguished</li> 
      <li>2:30 – main reactor hall roof fires suppressed</li> 
      <li>3:30 – arrival of Kiev firefighters<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>4:50 – fires mostly localized</li> 
      <li>6:35 – all fires extinguished<sup>‡</sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup></li> 
     </ul> 
     <p><sup>‡</sup>With the exception of the fire contained inside Reactor 4, which continued to burn for many days.<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:73</sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Announcement_and_evacuation">Announcement and evacuation</span></h4>

       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div>
