          <div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">
           Russian language announcement
          </div> 
         </div> 
         <hr></td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td> 
       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <p>The nearby city of Pripyat was not immediately evacuated. The townspeople went about their usual business, completely oblivious to what had just happened. However, within a few hours of the explosion, dozens of people fell ill. Later, they reported severe headaches and metallic tastes in their mouths, along with uncontrollable fits of coughing and vomiting.<sup id="cite_ref-TimeDisaster_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TimeDisaster-59">[57]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>As the plant was run by authorities in Moscow, the government of Ukraine did not receive prompt information on the accident.<sup id="cite_ref-shevchenko_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shevchenko-60">[58]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Valentyna_Shevchenko" title="Valentyna Shevchenko">Valentyna Shevchenko</a>, then Chairman of the Presidium of <a href="/wiki/Verkhovna_Rada" title="Verkhovna Rada">Verkhovna Rada</a> of the Ukrainian SSR, recalls that Ukraine's <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Internal_Affairs_(Ukraine)" title="Ministry of Internal Affairs (Ukraine)">Minister of Internal Affairs</a> <a href="/wiki/Vasyl_Durdynets" title="Vasyl Durdynets">Vasyl Durdynets</a> phoned her at work at 9&nbsp;am to report current affairs; only at the end of the conversation did he add that there had been a fire at the Chernobyl nuclear power plant, but it was extinguished and everything was fine. When Shevchenko asked "How are the people?", he replied that there was nothing to be concerned about: "Some are celebrating a wedding, others are gardening, and others are fishing in the <a href="/wiki/Pripyat_River" title="Pripyat River">Pripyat River</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-shevchenko_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shevchenko-60">[58]</a></sup> Shevchenko then spoke to <a href="/wiki/Volodymyr_Shcherbytsky" title="Volodymyr Shcherbytsky">Volodymyr Shcherbytsky</a>, Head of the Central Committee of the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Ukraine_(Soviet_Union)" title="Communist Party of Ukraine (Soviet Union)">CPU</a> and de facto head of state, who said he anticipated a delegation of the state commission headed by the deputy chairman of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Ministers_of_USSR" title="Council of Ministers of USSR" class="mw-redirect">Council of Ministers of USSR</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-shevchenko_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shevchenko-60">[58]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>A commission was set up the same day (26 April) to investigate the accident. It was headed by <a href="/wiki/Valery_Legasov" title="Valery Legasov">Valery Legasov</a>, First Deputy Director of the Kurchatov Institute of Atomic Energy, and included leading nuclear specialist <a href="/wiki/Evgeny_Velikhov" title="Evgeny Velikhov">Evgeny Velikhov</a>, hydro-meteorologist <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Izrael" title="Yuri Izrael">Yuri Izrael</a>, radiologist <a href="/w/index.php?title=Leonid_Ilyin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Leonid Ilyin (page does not exist)">Leonid Ilyin</a> and others. They flew to <a href="/wiki/Boryspil_International_Airport" title="Boryspil International Airport">Boryspil International Airport</a> and arrived at the power plant in the evening of 26 April.<sup id="cite_ref-shevchenko_60-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shevchenko-60">[58]</a></sup> By that time two people had already died and 52 were hospitalised. The delegation soon had ample evidence that the reactor was destroyed and extremely high levels of radiation had caused a number of cases of radiation exposure. In the early hours of 27 April, over 24 hours after the initial blast, they ordered the <a href="/wiki/Emergency_evacuation" title="Emergency evacuation">evacuation</a> of Pripyat. Initially it was decided to evacuate the population for three days; later this was made permanent.<sup id="cite_ref-shevchenko_60-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shevchenko-60">[58]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>By 11:00 on 27 April, buses had arrived in Pripyat to start the evacuation.<sup id="cite_ref-shevchenko_60-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shevchenko-60">[58]</a></sup> The evacuation began at 14:00. A translated excerpt of the evacuation announcement follows:<sup id="cite_ref-pripyat_evacuation_announcement_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pripyat_evacuation_announcement-61">[59]</a></sup></p> 
     <blockquote class="templatequote"> 
      <p>For the attention of the residents of Pripyat! The City Council informs you that due to the accident at Chernobyl Power Station in the city of Pripyat the radioactive conditions in the vicinity are deteriorating. The Communist Party, its officials and the armed forces are taking necessary steps to combat this. Nevertheless, with the view to keep people as safe and healthy as possible, the children being top priority, we need to temporarily evacuate the citizens in the nearest towns of Kiev Oblast. For these reasons, starting from April 27, 1986 2&nbsp;pm each apartment block will be able to have a bus at its disposal, supervised by the police and the city officials. It is highly advisable to take your documents, some vital personal belongings and a certain amount of food, just in case, with you. The senior executives of public and industrial facilities of the city has decided on the list of employees needed to stay in Pripyat to maintain these facilities in a good working order. All the houses will be guarded by the police during the evacuation period. Comrades, leaving your residences temporarily please make sure you have turned off the lights, electrical equipment and water and shut the windows. Please keep calm and orderly in the process of this short-term evacuation.</p> 
      <div class="templatequotecite">
       <cite>— Evacuation announcement in Pripyat, 27 April 1986 (14:00)</cite>
      </div> 
     </blockquote> 
     <p>To expedite the evacuation, residents were told to bring only what was necessary, and that it would only last approximately three days. As a result, most personal belongings were left behind, and remain there today. By 15:00, 53,000 people were evacuated to various villages of the <a href="/wiki/Kiev_Oblast" title="Kiev Oblast">Kiev region</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-shevchenko_60-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shevchenko-60">[58]</a></sup> The next day, talks began for evacuating people from the 10&nbsp;km zone.<sup id="cite_ref-shevchenko_60-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shevchenko-60">[58]</a></sup> Ten days after the accident, the evacuation area was expanded to 30&nbsp;km (19&nbsp;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesSocialImpact_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesSocialImpact-62">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:115,120–1</sup> This <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_Nuclear_Power_Plant_Exclusion_Zone" title="Chernobyl Nuclear Power Plant Exclusion Zone" class="mw-redirect">"exclusion zone"</a> has remained ever since, although its shape has changed and its size has been expanded.</p> 
     <p>These evacuations actually had some economic benefit, moving people to areas of labour shortage in Belarus and Ukraine.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesSocialImpact_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesSocialImpact-62">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:90</sup></p> 
     <p>Evacuation began long before the accident was publicly known throughout the Union. Only on 28 April, after radiation levels set off alarms at the <a href="/wiki/Forsmark_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Forsmark Nuclear Power Plant">Forsmark Nuclear Power Plant</a> in Sweden,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup> over 1,000 kilometres (620&nbsp;mi) from the Chernobyl Plant, did the Soviet Union publicly admit that an accident had occurred. At 21:02 that evening a 20-second announcement was read in the TV news program <i><a href="/wiki/Vremya" title="Vremya">Vremya</a></i>:<sup id="cite_ref-vremya_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vremya-64">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GalleryTimeline_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GalleryTimeline-65">[63]</a></sup></p> 
     <blockquote class="templatequote"> 
      <p>There has been an accident at the Chernobyl Nuclear Power Plant. One of the nuclear reactors was damaged. The effects of the accident are being remedied. Assistance has been provided for any affected people. An investigative commission has been set up.</p> 
      <div class="templatequotecite">
       <cite>— <i><a href="/wiki/Vremya" title="Vremya">Vremya</a></i>, 28 April 1986 (21:00)<sup id="cite_ref-vremya_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vremya-64">[62]</a></sup></cite>
      </div> 
     </blockquote> 
     <p>This was the entirety of the announcement of the accident. The <a href="/wiki/Telegraph_Agency_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Telegraph Agency of the Soviet Union">Telegraph Agency of the Soviet Union</a> (TASS) then discussed <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island</a> and other American nuclear accidents, an example of the common Soviet tactic of <a href="/wiki/Whataboutism" title="Whataboutism">emphasizing foreign disasters</a> when one occurred in the Soviet Union. The mention of a commission, however, indicated to observers the seriousness of the incident,<sup id="cite_ref-schmemann19860429_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schmemann19860429-66">[64]</a></sup> and subsequent state radio broadcasts were replaced with classical music, which was a common method of preparing the public for an announcement of a tragedy.<sup id="cite_ref-GalleryTimeline_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GalleryTimeline-65">[63]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Around the same time, <a href="/wiki/ABC_News" title="ABC News">ABC News</a> released its report about the disaster.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Shevchenko was the first of the Ukrainian state top officials to arrive at the disaster site early on 28 April. There she spoke with members of medical staff and people, who were calm and hopeful that they could soon return to their homes. Shevchenko returned home near midnight, stopping at a radiological checkpoint in Vilcha, one of the first that were set up soon after the accident.<sup id="cite_ref-shevchenko_60-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shevchenko-60">[58]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>There was a notification from Moscow that there was no reason to postpone the 1 May <a href="/wiki/International_Workers%27_Day" title="International Workers' Day">International Workers' Day</a> celebrations in Kiev (including the annual parade), but on 30 April a meeting of the Political bureau of the Central Committee of CP(b)U took place to discuss the plan for the upcoming celebration. Scientists were reporting that the radiological background in Kiev city was normal. At the meeting, which was finished at 18:00, it was decided to shorten celebrations from the regular 3.5–4 to under 2 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-shevchenko_60-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shevchenko-60">[58]</a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Steam_explosion_risk">Steam explosion risk</span></h4> 
     <p>Two floors of bubbler pools beneath the reactor served as a large water reservoir for the emergency cooling pumps and as a pressure suppression system capable of condensing steam in case of a small broken steam pipe; the third floor above them, below the reactor, served as a steam tunnel. The steam released by a broken pipe was supposed to enter the steam tunnel and be led into the pools to bubble through a layer of water. After the disaster, the pools and the basement were flooded because of ruptured cooling water pipes and accumulated firefighting water, and constituted a serious steam explosion risk.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:269px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Chernobyl_lava_flow.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7c/Chernobyl_lava_flow.jpg/267px-Chernobyl_lava_flow.jpg" width="267" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7c/Chernobyl_lava_flow.jpg/401px-Chernobyl_lava_flow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7c/Chernobyl_lava_flow.jpg/534px-Chernobyl_lava_flow.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="555"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Chernobyl_lava_flow.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Chernobyl 
        <a href="/wiki/Corium_(nuclear_reactor)" title="Corium (nuclear reactor)">corium lava</a> flows formed by fuel-containing mass in the basement of the plant
        <sup id="cite_ref-Lava1_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lava1-68">[66]</a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Smoldering" title="Smoldering" class="mw-redirect">smoldering</a> graphite, fuel and other material above, at more than 1200&nbsp;°C,<sup id="cite_ref-lava2_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lava2-69">[67]</a></sup> started to burn through the reactor floor and mixed with molten concrete from the reactor lining, creating <a href="/wiki/Corium_(nuclear_reactor)" title="Corium (nuclear reactor)">corium</a>, a radioactive semi-liquid material comparable to <a href="/wiki/Lava" title="Lava">lava</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lava1_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lava1-68">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lava3_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lava3-70">[68]</a></sup> If this mixture had melted through the floor into the pool of water, it was feared it could have created a serious steam explosion that would have ejected more radioactive material from the reactor. It became necessary to drain the pool.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The bubbler pool could be drained by opening its <a href="/wiki/Sluice_gates" title="Sluice gates" class="mw-redirect">sluice gates</a>. Volunteers in <a href="/wiki/Diving_suit" title="Diving suit">diving suits</a> entered the radioactive water and managed to open the gates. These were the engineers Alexei Ananenko (who knew where the valves were) and Valeri Bezpalov, accompanied by a third man, Boris Baranov, who provided them with light from a lamp, though his lamp failed, leaving them to find the valves by feeling their way along a pipe.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernobyl_1986.2C_p.178_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernobyl_1986.2C_p.178-72">[70]</a></sup> All of them returned to the surface and according to Ananenko, their colleagues jumped for joy when they heard they had managed to open the valves. Upon emerging from the water, the three were already suffering from <a href="/wiki/Radiation_sickness" title="Radiation sickness" class="mw-redirect">radiation sickness</a> and later died.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> Some sources claim incorrectly that they died in the plant.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernobyl_1986.2C_p.178_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernobyl_1986.2C_p.178-72">[70]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>It is likely that intense <a href="/wiki/Alpha_radiation" title="Alpha radiation" class="mw-redirect">alpha radiation</a> <a href="/wiki/Radiolysis" title="Radiolysis">hydrolyzed</a> the water, generating a low-<a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a> (H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) solution akin to an <a href="/wiki/Oxidizing_acid" title="Oxidizing acid">oxidizing acid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sattonnay_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sattonnay-74">[72]</a></sup> Conversion of bubbler pool water to H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is confirmed by the presence in the Chernobyl lavas of <a href="/wiki/Studtite" title="Studtite">studtite</a> and metastudtite,<sup id="cite_ref-studite1_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-studite1-75">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Studite2_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Studite2-76">[74]</a></sup> the only minerals that contain peroxide.<sup id="cite_ref-Studite3_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Studite3-77">[75]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Fire brigade pumps were then used to drain the basement. The operation was not completed until 8 May, after 20,000&nbsp;metric tons of highly radioactive water were pumped out.</p> 
     <p>With the bubbler pool gone, a meltdown was less likely to produce a powerful steam explosion. To do so, the molten core would now have to reach the <a href="/wiki/Water_table" title="Water table">water table</a> below the reactor. To reduce the likelihood of this, it was decided to freeze the earth beneath the reactor, which would also stabilize the foundations. Using <a href="/wiki/Well_drilling" title="Well drilling">oil drilling equipment</a>, the injection of liquid nitrogen began on 4 May. It was estimated that 25&nbsp;metric tons of liquid nitrogen per day would be required to keep the soil frozen at −100&nbsp;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:59</sup> This idea<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup> was soon scrapped and the bottom room where the cooling system would have been installed was filled with concrete.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Debris_removal">Debris removal</span></h4> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:269px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Chernobylreactor_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Chernobylreactor_1.jpg/267px-Chernobylreactor_1.jpg" width="267" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Chernobylreactor_1.jpg/401px-Chernobylreactor_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Chernobylreactor_1.jpg/534px-Chernobylreactor_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Chernobylreactor_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Chernobyl power plant in 2006 with the 
        <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_Nuclear_Power_Plant_sarcophagus" title="Chernobyl Nuclear Power Plant sarcophagus">sarcophagus containment structure</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The worst of the radioactive <a href="/wiki/Debris" title="Debris">debris</a> was collected inside what was left of the reactor, much of it shoveled in by <a href="/wiki/Liquidator_(Chernobyl)" title="Liquidator (Chernobyl)" class="mw-redirect">liquidators</a> wearing heavy protective gear (dubbed "bio-robots" by the military); these workers could only spend a maximum of 40&nbsp;seconds at a time working on the rooftops of the surrounding buildings because of the extremely high doses of radiation given off by the blocks of graphite and other debris. The reactor itself was covered with bags of sand, lead and <a href="/wiki/Boric_acid" title="Boric acid">boric acid</a> dropped from helicopters: some 5000 metric tons of material were dropped during the week that followed the accident.</p> 
     <p>At the time there was still fear that the reactor could re-enter a self-sustaining nuclear chain-reaction and explode again, and a new containment structure was planned to prevent rain entering and triggering such an explosion, and to prevent further release of radioactive material. This was the largest civil engineering task in history, involving a quarter of a million construction workers who all reached their official lifetime limits of radiation.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCContaining_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCContaining-54">[52]</a></sup> By December 1986, a large concrete <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_Nuclear_Power_Plant_sarcophagus" title="Chernobyl Nuclear Power Plant sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a> had been erected to seal off the reactor and its contents.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup> A unique "clean up" medal was given to the workers.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Many of the vehicles used by the liquidators remain parked in a field in the Chernobyl area.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>During the construction of the sarcophagus, a scientific team re-entered the reactor as part of an investigation dubbed "Complex Expedition", to locate and contain nuclear fuel in a way that could not lead to another explosion. These scientists manually collected cold fuel rods, but great heat was still emanating from the core. Rates of radiation in different parts of the building were monitored by drilling holes into the reactor and inserting long metal detector tubes. The scientists were exposed to high levels of radiation and radioactive dust.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCContaining_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCContaining-54">[52]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>After six months of investigation, in December 1986, they discovered with the help of a remote camera an intensely radioactive mass in the basement of Unit Four, more than two metres wide and weighing hundreds of tons, which they called "the elephant's foot" for its wrinkled appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> The mass was composed of sand, glass and a large amount of nuclear fuel that had escaped from the reactor. The concrete beneath the reactor was steaming hot, and was breached by solidified lava and spectacular unknown crystalline forms termed <a href="/wiki/Chernobylite" title="Chernobylite">chernobylite</a>. It was concluded that there was no further risk of explosion.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCContaining_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCContaining-54">[52]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Liquidators worked under deplorable conditions, poorly informed and with poor protections. Many if not most of them exceeded radiation safety limits.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesSocialImpact_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesSocialImpact-62">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:177–183</sup><sup id="cite_ref-PetrynaLifeExposed_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetrynaLifeExposed-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:2</sup> Some exceeded limits by over 100 times—leading to rapid death.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesSocialImpact_62-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesSocialImpact-62">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:187</sup></p> 
     <p>The official contaminated zones became stage to a massive clean-up effort lasting seven months.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesSocialImpact_62-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesSocialImpact-62">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:177–183</sup> The official reason for such early (and dangerous) decontamination efforts, rather than allowing time for natural decay, was that the land must be re-peopled and brought back into cultivation. Indeed, within fifteen months 75% of the land was under cultivation, even though only a third of the evacuated villages were resettled. Defense forces must have done much of the work. Yet this land was of marginal agricultural value. According to historian David Marples, the administration had a psychological purpose for the clean-up: they wished to forestall panic regarding nuclear energy, and even to restart the Chernobyl power station.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesSocialImpact_62-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesSocialImpact-62">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:78–9,87,192–3</sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span></h2> 
     <p>There were two official explanations of the accident.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operator_error">Operator error</span></h3> 
     <p>The first official explanation of the accident, later acknowledged to be erroneous, was published in August 1986. It effectively placed the blame on the <a href="/wiki/Power_plant" title="Power plant" class="mw-redirect">power plant</a> operators. To investigate the causes of the accident the <a href="/wiki/IAEA" title="IAEA" class="mw-redirect">IAEA</a> created a group known as the <a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Nuclear_Safety_Advisory_Group&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="International Nuclear Safety Advisory Group (page does not exist)">International Nuclear Safety Advisory Group</a> (INSAG), which in its report of 1986, INSAG-1, on the whole also supported this view, based on the data provided by the Soviets and the oral statements of specialists.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup> In this view, the catastrophic accident was caused by gross violations of operating rules and regulations. "During preparation and testing of the turbine generator under run-down conditions using the auxiliary load, personnel disconnected a series of technical protection systems and breached the most important operational safety provisions for conducting a technical exercise."<sup id="cite_ref-atom_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atom-85">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:311</sup></p> 
     <p>The operator error was probably due to their lack of knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor_physics" title="Nuclear reactor physics">nuclear reactor physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_engineering" title="Nuclear engineering">engineering</a>, as well as lack of experience and training. According to these allegations, at the time of the accident the reactor was being operated with many key safety systems turned off, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Core_Cooling_System" title="Emergency Core Cooling System" class="mw-redirect">Emergency Core Cooling System</a> (ECCS), LAR (Local Automatic control system), and AZ (emergency power reduction system). Personnel had an insufficiently detailed understanding of technical procedures involved with the nuclear reactor, and knowingly ignored regulations to speed test completion.<sup id="cite_ref-atom_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atom-85">[83]</a></sup></p> 
     <blockquote class="templatequote"> 
      <p>The developers of the reactor plant considered this combination of events to be impossible and therefore did not allow for the creation of emergency protection systems capable of preventing the combination of events that led to the crisis, namely the intentional disabling of emergency protection equipment plus the violation of operating procedures. Thus the primary cause of the accident was the extremely improbable combination of rule infringement plus the operational routine allowed by the power station staff.<sup id="cite_ref-atom_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atom-85">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:312</sup></p> 
     </blockquote> 
     <p>In this analysis of the causes of the accident, deficiencies in the reactor design and in the operating regulations that made the accident possible were set aside and mentioned only casually. Serious critical observations covered only general questions and did not address the specific reasons for the accident. The following general picture arose from these observations. Several procedural irregularities also helped to make the accident possible. One was insufficient communication between the safety officers and the operators in charge of the experiment being run that night.</p> 
     <p>The reactor operators disabled safety systems down to the generators, which the test was really about. The main process computer, SKALA, was running in such a way that the main control computer could not shut down the reactor or even reduce power. Normally the reactor would have started to insert all of the <a href="/wiki/Control_rods" title="Control rods" class="mw-redirect">control rods</a>. The computer would have also started the "Emergency Core Protection System" that introduces 24 control rods into the active zone within 2.5 seconds, which is still slow by 1986 standards. All control was transferred from the process computer to the human operators.</p> 
     <p>On the subject of the disconnection of safety systems, Valery Legasov said, in 1987, that "[i]t was like airplane pilots experimenting with the engines in flight".<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-56">[54]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>This view is reflected in numerous publications and also artistic works on the theme of the Chernobyl accident that appeared immediately after the accident,<sup id="cite_ref-MedvedevZ_26-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedvedevZ-26">[25]</a></sup> and for a long time remained dominant in the public consciousness and in popular publications.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operating_instructions_and_design_deficiencies_found">Operating instructions and design deficiencies found</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:269px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Chaes_cz_1-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Chaes_cz_1-2.jpg/267px-Chaes_cz_1-2.jpg" width="267" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Chaes_cz_1-2.jpg/401px-Chaes_cz_1-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Chaes_cz_1-2.jpg/534px-Chaes_cz_1-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="746" data-file-height="563"></a>

     <div class="thumb tright">

        <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">moderated</a>, and therefore increased the fission rate in the beginning of the rod insertion 4. No reinforced 
        <a href="/wiki/Containment_building" title="Containment building">containment building</a>.

       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:269px;">

        <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">moderator</a> ejected from the core. The largest lump shows an intact 
        <a href="/wiki/Control_rod" title="Control rod">control rod</a> channel.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Ukraine has declassified a number of <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a> documents from period between 1971 and 1988 related to the Chernobyl plant, mentioning for example previous reports of structural damages caused by negligence during construction of the plant (such as splitting of concrete layers) that were never acted upon. They document over 29 emergency situations in the plant during this period, 8 of which were caused by negligence or poor competence on the part of personnel.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1991 a Commission of the USSR State Committee for the Supervision of Safety in Industry and Nuclear Power has reassessed the causes and circumstances of the Chernobyl accident and came to new insights and conclusions. Based on it, in 1992 the IAEA Nuclear Safety Advisory Group (INSAG) published an additional report, INSAG-7,<sup id="cite_ref-insag7_30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-insag7-30">[29]</a></sup> which reviewed "that part of the INSAG-1 report in which primary attention is given to the reasons for the accident". and included the USSR State Commission report as Appendix I.<sup id="cite_ref-insag7_30-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-insag7-30">[29]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In this INSAG report, most of the earlier accusations against staff for breach of regulations were acknowledged to be either erroneous, based on incorrect information obtained in August 1986, or less relevant. This report reflected another view of the main reasons for the accident, presented in Appendix I. According to this account, the operators' actions in turning off the Emergency Core Cooling System, interfering with the settings on the protection equipment, and blocking the level and pressure in the separator drum did not contribute to the original cause of the accident and its magnitude, although they may have been a breach of regulations. Turning off the emergency system designed to prevent the two turbine generators from stopping was not a violation of regulations.<sup id="cite_ref-insag7_30-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-insag7-30">[29]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Human factors contributed to the conditions that led to the disaster. These included operating the reactor at a low power level—less than 700&nbsp;MW—a level documented in the run-down test program, and operating with a small <a href="/wiki/Reactivity_(nuclear)" title="Reactivity (nuclear)" class="mw-redirect">operational reactivity margin</a> (ORM). The 1986 assertions of Soviet experts notwithstanding, regulations did not prohibit operating the reactor at this low power level.<sup id="cite_ref-insag7_30-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-insag7-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:18</sup></p> 
     <p>However, regulations did forbid operating the reactor with a small margin of reactivity. Yet "post-accident studies have shown that the way in which the real role of the ORM is reflected in the Operating Procedures and design documentation for the RBMK-1000 is extremely contradictory", and furthermore, "ORM was not treated as an operational safety limit, violation of which could lead to an accident".<sup id="cite_ref-insag7_30-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-insag7-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:34–25</sup></p> 
     <p>According to the INSAG-7 Report, the chief reasons for the accident lie in the peculiarities of physics and in the construction of the reactor. There are two such reasons:<sup id="cite_ref-insag7_30-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-insag7-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:18</sup></p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>The reactor had a dangerously large positive <a href="/wiki/Void_coefficient_of_reactivity" title="Void coefficient of reactivity" class="mw-redirect">void coefficient of reactivity</a>. The void coefficient is a measurement of how a reactor responds to increased steam formation in the water coolant. Most other reactor designs have a negative coefficient, i.e. the nuclear reaction rate slows when steam bubbles form in the coolant, since as the vapor phase in the reactor increases, fewer <a href="/wiki/Neutrons" title="Neutrons" class="mw-redirect">neutrons</a> are slowed down. <a href="/wiki/Fast_neutron" title="Fast neutron" class="mw-redirect">Faster neutrons</a> are less likely to split <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> atoms, so the reactor produces less power (a negative feedback). Chernobyl's RBMK reactor, however, used solid <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_graphite" title="Nuclear graphite">graphite</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">neutron moderator</a> to <a href="/wiki/Thermal_neutrons" title="Thermal neutrons" class="mw-redirect">slow down the neutrons</a>, and the water in it, on the contrary, acts like a harmful <a href="/wiki/Neutron_absorber" title="Neutron absorber" class="mw-redirect">neutron absorber</a>. Thus neutrons are slowed down even if steam bubbles form in the water. Furthermore, because steam <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">absorbs neutrons</a> much less readily than water, increasing the intensity of vaporization means that more neutrons are able to split uranium atoms, increasing the reactor's power output. This makes the RBMK design very unstable at low power levels, and prone to suddenly increasing energy production to a dangerous level. This behavior is <a href="/wiki/Counter-intuitive" title="Counter-intuitive" class="mw-redirect">counter-intuitive</a>, and this property of the reactor was unknown to the crew.</li> 
      <li>A more significant flaw was in the design of the <a href="/wiki/Control_rods" title="Control rods" class="mw-redirect">control rods</a> that are inserted into the reactor to slow down the reaction. In the RBMK reactor design, the lower part of each control rod was made of graphite and was 1.3 meters shorter than necessary, and in the space beneath the rods were hollow channels filled with water. The upper part of the rod, the truly functional part that absorbs the neutrons and thereby halts the reaction, was made of <a href="/wiki/Boron_carbide" title="Boron carbide">boron carbide</a>. With this design, when the rods are inserted into the reactor from the uppermost position, the graphite parts initially displace some water (which absorbs neutrons, as mentioned above), effectively causing fewer neutrons to be absorbed initially. Thus for the first few seconds of control rod activation, reactor power output is increased, rather than reduced as desired. This behavior is counter-intuitive and was not known to the reactor operators.</li> 
      <li>Other deficiencies besides these were noted in the RBMK-1000 reactor design, as were its non-compliance with accepted standards and with the requirements of nuclear reactor safety.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis_of_views">Analysis of views</span></h3> 
     <p>Both views were heavily <a href="/wiki/Lobbied" title="Lobbied" class="mw-redirect">lobbied</a> by different groups, including the reactor's designers, power plant personnel, and the Soviet and Ukrainian governments. According to the IAEA's 1986 analysis, the main cause of the accident was the operators' actions. But according to the IAEA's 1993 revised analysis the main cause was the reactor's design.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> One reason there were such contradictory viewpoints and so much debate about the causes of the Chernobyl accident was that the primary data covering the disaster, as registered by the instruments and sensors, were not completely published in the official sources.</p> 
     <p>Once again, the <a href="/wiki/Human_factor" title="Human factor" class="mw-redirect">human factor</a> had to be considered as a major element in causing the accident. INSAG notes that both the operating regulations and staff handled the disabling of the reactor protection easily enough: witness the length of time for which the ECCS was out of service while the reactor was operated at half power. INSAG's view is that it was the operating crew's deviation from the test program that was mostly to blame. "Most reprehensibly, unapproved changes in the test procedure were deliberately made on the spot, although the plant was known to be in a very different condition from that intended for the test."<sup id="cite_ref-insag7_30-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-insag7-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:24</sup></p> 
     <p>As in the previously released report INSAG-1, close attention is paid in report INSAG-7 to the inadequate (at the moment of the accident) "culture of safety" at all levels. Deficiency in the safety culture was inherent not only at the operational stage but also, and to no lesser extent, during activities at other stages in the lifetime of nuclear power plants (including design, engineering, construction, manufacture, and regulation). The poor quality of operating procedures and instructions, and their conflicting character, put a heavy burden on the operating crew, including the Chief Engineer. "The accident can be said to have flowed from a deficient safety culture, not only at the Chernobyl plant, but throughout the Soviet design, operating and regulatory organizations for nuclear power that existed at that time."<sup id="cite_ref-insag7_30-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-insag7-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:24</sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster_effects" title="Chernobyl disaster effects" class="mw-redirect">Chernobyl disaster effects</a>
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_and_international_spread_of_radioactive_substances">National and international spread of radioactive substances</span></h3> 
     <p>Four hundred times more radioactive material was released from Chernobyl than by the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">atomic bombing</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a>. The disaster released 1/100 to 1/1000 of the total amount of radioactivity released by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_testing" title="Nuclear weapons testing">nuclear weapons testing</a> during the 1950s and 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup> Approximately 100,000&nbsp;km² of land was significantly contaminated with fallout, with the worst hit regions being in Belarus, Ukraine and Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesDecade_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesDecade-91">[89]</a></sup> Slighter levels of contamination were detected over all of Europe except for the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-torch_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-torch-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RFI_24_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFI_24-92">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The initial evidence that a major release of radioactive material was affecting other countries came not from Soviet sources, but from Sweden. On the morning of 28 April<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup> workers at the Forsmark Nuclear Power Plant (approximately 1,100&nbsp;km (680&nbsp;mi) from the Chernobyl site) were found to have radioactive particles on their clothes.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>It was Sweden's search for the source of radioactivity, after they had determined there was no leak at the Swedish plant, that at noon on 28 April led to the first hint of a serious nuclear problem in the western Soviet Union. Hence the evacuation of Pripyat on 27 April 36 hours after the initial explosions, was silently completed before the disaster became known outside the Soviet Union. The rise in radiation levels had at that time already been measured in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, but a civil service strike delayed the response and publication.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup></p>

        <td>0.1</td> 
        <td>—</td> 
        <td>—</td> 
        <td>—</td> 
        <td>—</td> 
        <td>—</td> 
        <td>—</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr style="background:#b3f4bb;"> 
        <td><a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a></td> 
        <td>60</td> 
        <td>0.2</td> 
        <td>—</td>

       <tr> 
        <th>Totals</th> 
        <th colspan="2">162,160&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup></th> 
        <th colspan="2">19,100&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup></th> 
        <th colspan="2">7,200&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup></th> 
        <th colspan="2">3,100&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup></th> 
       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <p>Contamination from the Chernobyl accident was scattered irregularly depending on weather conditions, much of it deposited on mountainous regions such as the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Welsh</a> mountains and the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Highlands" title="Scottish Highlands">Scottish Highlands</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Adiabatic_process" title="Adiabatic process">adiabatic cooling</a> caused radioactive rainfall. The resulting patches of contamination were often highly localised, and water-flows across the ground contributed further to large variations in radioactivity over small areas. Sweden and Norway also received heavy fallout when the contaminated air collided with a cold front, bringing rain.<sup id="cite_ref-GouldFire_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GouldFire-98">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:43–44, 78</sup></p> 
     <p>Rain was purposely <a href="/wiki/Cloud_seeding" title="Cloud seeding">seeded</a> over 10,000&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> of the <a href="/wiki/Belorussian_SSR" title="Belorussian SSR" class="mw-redirect">Belorussian SSR</a> by the Soviet air force to remove radioactive particles from clouds heading toward highly populated areas. Heavy, black-coloured rain fell on the city of <a href="/wiki/Gomel" title="Gomel">Gomel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup> Reports from Soviet and Western scientists indicate that Belarus received about 60% of the contamination that fell on the former Soviet Union. However, the 2006 TORCH report stated that half of the volatile particles had landed outside Ukraine, Belarus, and Russia. A large area in Russia south of <a href="/wiki/Bryansk" title="Bryansk">Bryansk</a> was also contaminated, as were parts of northwestern Ukraine. Studies in surrounding countries indicate that over one million people could have been affected by radiation.<sup id="cite_ref-WNA-Chernobyl_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WNA-Chernobyl-100">[98]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Recently published data from a long-term monitoring program (The Korma Report)<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup> shows a decrease in internal <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">radiation exposure</a> of the inhabitants of a region in Belarus close to Gomel. Resettlement may even be possible in prohibited areas provided that people comply with appropriate dietary rules.</p> 
     <p>In Western Europe, precautionary measures taken in response to the radiation included seemingly arbitrary regulations banning the importation of certain foods but not others. In France some officials stated that the Chernobyl accident had no adverse effects.<sup id="cite_ref-FrenchCom_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FrenchCom-102">[100]</a></sup> Official figures in southern <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a> in Germany indicated that some wild plant species contained substantial levels of caesium, which were believed to have been passed onto them by wild boars, a significant number of which had already contained radioactive particles above the allowed level, consuming them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="slightly awkward English, suggest clarification of the radioactivity relationship between plants and boars (March 2011)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Kiev-UkrainianNationalChernobylMuseum_15.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Kiev-UkrainianNationalChernobylMuseum_15.jpg/300px-Kiev-UkrainianNationalChernobylMuseum_15.jpg" width="300" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Kiev-UkrainianNationalChernobylMuseum_15.jpg/450px-Kiev-UkrainianNationalChernobylMuseum_15.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Kiev-UkrainianNationalChernobylMuseum_15.jpg/600px-Kiev-UkrainianNationalChernobylMuseum_15.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Kiev-UkrainianNationalChernobylMuseum_15.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Piglet with 
        <a href="/wiki/Dipygus" title="Dipygus">dipygus</a> on exhibit at the 
        <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_National_Chornobyl_Museum" title="Ukrainian National Chornobyl Museum">Ukrainian National Chornobyl Museum</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Mutations in both humans<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and other animals increased following the disaster. On farms in <a href="/wiki/Narodychi_Raion" title="Narodychi Raion">Narodychi Raion</a> of Ukraine, for instance, in the first four years of the disaster nearly 350 animals were born with gross deformities such as missing or extra limbs, missing eyes, heads or ribs, or deformed skulls; in comparison, only three abnormal births had been registered in the five years prior.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup> Despite these claims, the World Health Organization states, "children conceived before or after their father's exposure showed no statistically significant differences in mutation frequencies".<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radioactive_release">Radioactive release</span></h3> 
     <p>Like many <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_and_reactor_accidents" title="Nuclear fuel and reactor accidents" class="mw-redirect">other releases of radioactivity</a> into the environment, the Chernobyl release was controlled by the physical and chemical properties of the radioactive elements in the core. Particularly dangerous are the highly radioactive <a href="/wiki/Fission_products" title="Fission products" class="mw-redirect">fission products</a>, those with high <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decay" title="Nuclear decay" class="mw-redirect">nuclear decay</a> rates that accumulate in the food chain, such as some of the isotopes of <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strontium" title="Strontium">strontium</a>. Iodine-131 and caesium-137 are responsible for most of the radiation exposure received by the general population.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD02-Ch2_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD02-Ch2-111">[109]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Detailed reports on the release of radioisotopes from the site were published in 1989<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup> and 1995,<sup id="cite_ref-OECD1995_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD1995-113">[111]</a></sup> with the latter report updated in 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD02-Ch2_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD02-Ch2-111">[109]</a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:AirDoseChernobylVector.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/AirDoseChernobylVector.svg/300px-AirDoseChernobylVector.svg.png" width="300" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/AirDoseChernobylVector.svg/450px-AirDoseChernobylVector.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/AirDoseChernobylVector.svg/600px-AirDoseChernobylVector.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="640"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:AirDoseChernobylVector.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Contributions of the various isotopes to the external (atmospheric) 
        <a href="/wiki/Absorbed_dose" title="Absorbed dose">absorbed dose</a> in the contaminated area of Pripyat, from soon after the accident, to years after the accident.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Totalexternaldoseratecher.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Totalexternaldoseratecher.png/300px-Totalexternaldoseratecher.png" width="300" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Totalexternaldoseratecher.png/450px-Totalexternaldoseratecher.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Totalexternaldoseratecher.png/600px-Totalexternaldoseratecher.png 2x" data-file-width="911" data-file-height="623"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Totalexternaldoseratecher.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Equivalent_dose" title="Equivalent dose">external relative gamma dose</a> for a person in the open near the Chernobyl disaster site.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>At different times after the accident, different <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> were responsible for the majority of the external dose. The activity of any radioisotope, and therefore the quantity of that isotope remaining, after 7 decay <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-lives</a> have passed, is less than 1% of its initial magnitude,<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup> and it continues to reduce beyond 0.78% after 7 half-lives to 0.098% remaining after 10 half-lives have passed and so on.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[114]</a></sup> The release of radioisotopes from the nuclear fuel was largely controlled by their <a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">boiling points</a>, and the majority of the <a href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity" class="mw-redirect">radioactivity</a> present in the core was retained in the reactor.</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>All of the <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gases</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Krypton" title="Krypton">krypton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a>, contained within the reactor were released immediately into the atmosphere by the first steam explosion.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD02-Ch2_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD02-Ch2-111">[109]</a></sup> The atmospheric release of <a href="/wiki/Xenon-133" title="Xenon-133" class="mw-redirect">xenon-133</a>, with a half-life of 5 days, is estimated at 5200&nbsp;PBq.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD02-Ch2_111-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD02-Ch2-111">[109]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>50 to 60% of all core <a href="/wiki/Radioiodine" title="Radioiodine" class="mw-redirect">radioiodine</a> in the reactor, about 1760&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">PBq</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7018176000000000000♠</span>1760<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em">×</span>10<sup>15</sup>&nbsp;becquerels</span>), or about 0.4&nbsp;kg, was released, as a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimed</a> <a href="/wiki/Vapor" title="Vapor">vapor</a>, solid particles, and <a href="/wiki/Organoiodine_compound" title="Organoiodine compound">organic iodine</a> <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">compounds</a>. Iodine-131 has a half-life of 8 days.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD02-Ch2_111-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD02-Ch2-111">[109]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>20 to 40% of all core <a href="/wiki/Caesium-137" title="Caesium-137">caesium-137</a> was released, 85&nbsp;PBq in all.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD02-Ch2_111-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD02-Ch2-111">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[115]</a></sup> Caesium was released in <a href="/wiki/Particulate" title="Particulate" class="mw-redirect">aerosol</a> form; caesium-137, along with <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_strontium" title="Isotopes of strontium">isotopes of strontium</a>, are the two primary elements preventing the Chernobyl exclusion zone being re-inhabited.<sup id="cite_ref-stanford1_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanford1-118">[116]</a></sup> <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7016850000000000000♠</span>8.5<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em">×</span>10<sup>16</sup>&nbsp;Bq</span> equals 24 kilograms of caesium-137.<sup id="cite_ref-stanford1_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanford1-118">[116]</a></sup> Cs-137 has a half-life of 30 years.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD02-Ch2_111-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD02-Ch2-111">[109]</a></sup></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Tellurium-132" title="Tellurium-132" class="mw-redirect">Tellurium-132</a>, half-life 78 hours, an estimated 1150&nbsp;PBq was released.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD02-Ch2_111-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD02-Ch2-111">[109]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>An early estimate for total <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">nuclear fuel</a> material released to the environment was <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7000300000000000000♠</span>3<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em">±</span>1.5</span>%; this was later revised to <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7000350000000000000♠</span>3.5<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em">±</span>0.5</span>%. This corresponds to the atmospheric emission of 6&nbsp;t of fragmented fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD1995_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD1995-113">[111]</a></sup></li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>Two sizes of particles were released: small particles of 0.3 to 1.5 micrometers (<a href="/wiki/Aerodynamic" title="Aerodynamic" class="mw-redirect">aerodynamic</a> diameter) and large particles of 10 micrometers. The large particles contained about 80% to 90% of the released nonvolatile radioisotopes <a href="/wiki/Zirconium-95" title="Zirconium-95" class="mw-redirect">zirconium-95</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niobium-95" title="Niobium-95" class="mw-redirect">niobium-95</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lanthanum-140" title="Lanthanum-140" class="mw-redirect">lanthanum-140</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cerium-144" title="Cerium-144" class="mw-redirect">cerium-144</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Transuranic_elements" title="Transuranic elements" class="mw-redirect">transuranic elements</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">neptunium</a>, plutonium and the <a href="/wiki/Minor_actinides" title="Minor actinides" class="mw-redirect">minor actinides</a>, embedded in a <a href="/wiki/Uranium_oxide" title="Uranium oxide">uranium oxide</a> matrix.</p> 
     <p>The dose that was calculated is the <a href="/wiki/Equivalent_dose" title="Equivalent dose">relative</a> external gamma dose rate for a person standing in the open. The exact dose to a person in the real world who would spend most of their time sleeping indoors in a <a href="/wiki/Fallout_shelter" title="Fallout shelter">shelter</a> and then venturing out to consume an <a href="/wiki/Internal_dose" title="Internal dose" class="mw-redirect">internal dose</a> from the inhalation or ingestion of a <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope" title="Radioisotope" class="mw-redirect">radioisotope</a>, requires a personnel specific <a href="/wiki/Radiation_dose_reconstruction" title="Radiation dose reconstruction">radiation dose reconstruction</a> analysis.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Residual_radioactivity_in_the_environment">Residual radioactivity in the environment</span></h3> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rivers.2C_lakes_and_reservoirs">Rivers, lakes and reservoirs</span></h4> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Chernobyl,_Ukraine.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Chernobyl%2C_Ukraine.jpg/350px-Chernobyl%2C_Ukraine.jpg" width="350" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Chernobyl%2C_Ukraine.jpg/525px-Chernobyl%2C_Ukraine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Chernobyl%2C_Ukraine.jpg/700px-Chernobyl%2C_Ukraine.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2000"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Chernobyl,_Ukraine.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Earth_Observing-1" title="Earth Observing-1">Earth Observing-1</a> image of the reactor and surrounding area in April 2009
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The Chernobyl nuclear power plant is located next to the Pripyat River, which feeds into the Dnieper reservoir system, one of the largest surface water systems in Europe, which at the time supplied water to Kiev's 2.4 million residents, and was still in spring flood when the accident occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesImpact_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesImpact-119">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:60</sup> The radioactive contamination of aquatic systems therefore became a major problem in the immediate aftermath of the accident.<sup id="cite_ref-smithber05_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smithber05-120">[118]</a></sup> In the most affected areas of Ukraine, levels of radioactivity (particularly from radionuclides <sup>131</sup>I, <sup>137</sup>Cs and <sup>90</sup>Sr) in drinking water caused concern during the weeks and months after the accident,<sup id="cite_ref-smithber05_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smithber05-120">[118]</a></sup> though officially it was stated that all contaminants had settled to the bottom "in an insoluble phase" and would not dissolve for 800–1000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesImpact_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesImpact-119">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:64</sup> Guidelines for levels of radioiodine in drinking water were temporarily raised to 3,700&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">Bq</a>/L, allowing most water to be reported as safe,<sup id="cite_ref-smithber05_120-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smithber05-120">[118]</a></sup> and a year after the accident it was announced that even the water of the Chernobyl plant's cooling pond was within acceptable norms. Despite this, two months after the disaster the Kiev water supply was abruptly switched from the Dnieper to the Desna River.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesImpact_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesImpact-119">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:64–5</sup> Meanwhile, massive silt traps were constructed, along with an enormous 30m-deep underground barrier to prevent groundwater from the destroyed reactor entering the Pripyat River.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesImpact_119-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesImpact-119">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:65–67</sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Bio-accumulation" title="Bio-accumulation" class="mw-redirect">Bio-accumulation</a> of radioactivity in fish<sup id="cite_ref-kryshev95_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kryshev95-121">[119]</a></sup> resulted in concentrations (both in western Europe and in the former Soviet Union) that in many cases were significantly above guideline maximum levels for consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-smithber05_120-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smithber05-120">[118]</a></sup> Guideline maximum levels for radiocaesium in fish vary from country to country but are approximately 1000&nbsp;Bq/kg in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-euregs_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euregs-122">[120]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Kiev_Reservoir" title="Kiev Reservoir">Kiev Reservoir</a> in Ukraine, concentrations in fish were several thousand Bq/kg during the years after the accident.<sup id="cite_ref-kryshev95_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kryshev95-121">[119]</a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Chernobyl_radiation_map_1996.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Chernobyl_radiation_map_1996.svg/350px-Chernobyl_radiation_map_1996.svg.png" width="350" height="370" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Chernobyl_radiation_map_1996.svg/525px-Chernobyl_radiation_map_1996.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Chernobyl_radiation_map_1996.svg/700px-Chernobyl_radiation_map_1996.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1398" data-file-height="1476"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Chernobyl_radiation_map_1996.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Map of radiation levels in 1996 around Chernobyl.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In small <a href="/wiki/Closed_lake" title="Closed lake" class="mw-redirect">"closed" lakes</a> in Belarus and the Bryansk region of Russia, concentrations in a number of fish species varied from 100 to 60,000&nbsp;Bq/kg during the period 1990–92.<sup id="cite_ref-fleishman94_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fleishman94-123">[121]</a></sup> The contamination of fish caused short-term concern in parts of the UK and Germany and in the long term (years rather than months) in the affected areas of Ukraine, Belarus, and Russia as well as in parts of Scandinavia.<sup id="cite_ref-smithber05_120-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smithber05-120">[118]</a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Groundwater">Groundwater</span></h4> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">Groundwater</a> was not badly affected by the Chernobyl accident since <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radionuclides</a> with short half-lives decayed away long before they could affect groundwater supplies, and longer-lived radionuclides such as radiocaesium and radiostrontium were <a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">adsorbed</a> to surface <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soils</a> before they could transfer to groundwater.<sup id="cite_ref-iaea06_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iaea06-124">[122]</a></sup> However, significant transfers of radionuclides to groundwater have occurred from <a href="/wiki/Waste_disposal" title="Waste disposal" class="mw-redirect">waste disposal</a> sites in the 30&nbsp;km (19&nbsp;mi) exclusion zone around Chernobyl. Although there is a potential for transfer of radionuclides from these disposal sites off-site (i.e. out of the 30&nbsp;km (19&nbsp;mi) exclusion zone), the IAEA Chernobyl Report<sup id="cite_ref-iaea06_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iaea06-124">[122]</a></sup> argues that this is not significant in comparison to current levels of <a href="/wiki/Washout" title="Washout">washout</a> of surface-deposited radioactivity.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora_and_fauna">Flora and fauna</span></h4> 
     <p>After the disaster, four square kilometers of <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pine</a> forest directly downwind of the reactor turned reddish-brown and died, earning the name of the "<a href="/wiki/Red_Forest" title="Red Forest">Red Forest</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-bbcmulvey_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcmulvey-125">[123]</a></sup> Some animals in the worst-hit areas also died or stopped reproducing. Most <a href="/wiki/Domestic_animal" title="Domestic animal" class="mw-redirect">domestic animals</a> were removed from the exclusion zone, but horses left on an island in the Pripyat River 6&nbsp;km (4&nbsp;mi) from the power plant died when their <a href="/wiki/Thyroid" title="Thyroid">thyroid</a> glands were destroyed by radiation doses of 150–200&nbsp;Sv.<sup id="cite_ref-iaea1991_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iaea1991-126">[124]</a></sup> Some cattle on the same island died and those that survived were stunted because of thyroid damage. The next generation appeared to be normal.<sup id="cite_ref-iaea1991_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iaea1991-126">[124]</a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Red_Forest_Hill.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Red_Forest_Hill.jpg/225px-Red_Forest_Hill.jpg" width="225" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Red_Forest_Hill.jpg/338px-Red_Forest_Hill.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Red_Forest_Hill.jpg/450px-Red_Forest_Hill.jpg 2x" data-file-width="679" data-file-height="857"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Red_Forest_Hill.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> After the disaster, four square kilometers of 
        <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pine</a> forest directly downwind of the reactor turned reddish-brown and died, earning the name of the "
        <a href="/wiki/Red_Forest" title="Red Forest">Red Forest</a>".
        <sup id="cite_ref-bbcmulvey_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcmulvey-125">[123]</a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>A robot sent into the reactor itself has returned with samples of black, <a href="/wiki/Melanin" title="Melanin">melanin</a>-rich <a href="/wiki/Radiotrophic_fungus" title="Radiotrophic fungus">radiotrophic fungi</a> that are growing on the reactor's walls.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Of the 440,350 wild boar killed in the 2010 hunting season in Germany, over 1000 were found to be contaminated with levels of radiation above the permitted limit of 600 becquerels per kilogram, due to residual radioactivity from Chernobyl.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The Norwegian Agricultural Authority reported that in 2009 a total of 18,000 livestock in Norway needed to be given uncontaminated feed for a period of time before slaughter in order to ensure that their meat was safe for human consumption. This was due to residual radioactivity from Chernobyl in the plants they graze on in the wild during the summer. 1,914 sheep needed to be given uncontaminated feed for a period of time before slaughter during 2012, and these sheep were located in just 18 of Norway's municipalities, a decrease of 17 from the 35 municipalities affected animals were located in during 2011 (117 municipalities were affected during 1986).<sup id="cite_ref-thelocal1_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thelocal1-129">[127]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The after-effects of Chernobyl were expected to be seen for a further 100 years, although the severity of the effects would decline over that period.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup> Scientists report this is due to radioactive <a href="/wiki/Caesium-137" title="Caesium-137">caesium-137</a> isotopes being taken up by fungi such as <i><a href="/wiki/Cortinarius_caperatus" title="Cortinarius caperatus">Cortinarius caperatus</a></i> which is in turn eaten by sheep whilst grazing.<sup id="cite_ref-thelocal1_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thelocal1-129">[127]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The United Kingdom was forced to restrict the movement of sheep from upland areas when radioactive <a href="/wiki/Caesium-137" title="Caesium-137">caesium-137</a> fell across parts of Northern Ireland, Wales, Scotland and northern England. In the immediate aftermath of the disaster in 1986, a total of 4,225,000 sheep had their movement restricted across a total of 9,700 farms, in order to prevent contaminated meat entering the human food chain.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian-2009_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-2009-131">[129]</a></sup> The number of sheep and the number of farms affected has decreased since 1986, Northern Ireland was released from all restrictions in 2000 and by 2009 369 farms containing around 190,000 sheep remained under the restrictions in Wales, Cumbria and northern Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian-2009_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-2009-131">[129]</a></sup> The restrictions applying in Scotland were lifted in 2010, whilst those applying to Wales and Cumbria were lifted during 2012, meaning no farms in the UK remain restricted because of Chernobyl fallout.<sup id="cite_ref-Indy-Scot-2012_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indy-Scot-2012-132">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC-June-2012_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-June-2012-133">[131]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The legislation used to control sheep movement and compensate farmers (farmers were latterly compensated per animal to cover additional costs in holding animals prior to radiation monitoring) was revoked during October and November 2012 by the relevant authorities in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-UKFSA-01_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKFSA-01-134">[132]</a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:269px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Devant_OMS_5.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Devant_OMS_5.jpg/267px-Devant_OMS_5.jpg" width="267" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Devant_OMS_5.jpg/401px-Devant_OMS_5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Devant_OMS_5.jpg/534px-Devant_OMS_5.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2983" data-file-height="2383"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Devant_OMS_5.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Demonstration on Chernobyl day near 
        <a href="/wiki/WHO" title="WHO" class="mw-redirect">WHO</a> in Geneva
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_impact">Human impact</span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster_effects" title="Chernobyl disaster effects" class="mw-redirect">Chernobyl disaster effects</a>
     </div> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Deaths_due_to_the_Chernobyl_disaster" title="Deaths due to the Chernobyl disaster">Deaths due to the Chernobyl disaster</a>
     </div> 
     <p>In the aftermath of the accident, 237 people suffered from <a href="/wiki/Acute_radiation_sickness" title="Acute radiation sickness" class="mw-redirect">acute radiation sickness</a> (ARS), of whom 31 died within the first three months.<sup id="cite_ref-Hallenbeck_1994_15_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hallenbeck_1994_15-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup> Most of the victims were fire and rescue workers trying to bring the accident under control, who were not fully aware of how dangerous the exposure to <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Smoke" title="Smoke">smoke</a> was.</p> 
     <p>In 2005 the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_Forum" title="Chernobyl Forum">Chernobyl Forum</a>, composed of the <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">IAEA</a>, other UN organizations and the governments of Belarus, Russia and Ukraine, published a report on the radiological environmental and health consequences of the Chernobyl accident.</p> 
     <p>On the death toll of the accident, the report states that twenty-eight emergency workers ("liquidators") died from <a href="/wiki/Acute_radiation_syndrome" title="Acute radiation syndrome">acute radiation syndrome</a> including <a href="/wiki/Beta_burn" title="Beta burn" class="mw-redirect">beta burns</a> and 15 patients died from thyroid cancer in the following years, and it roughly estimated that cancer deaths caused by Chernobyl may reach a total of about 4000 among the 5&nbsp;million persons residing in the contaminated areas, the report projected cancer mortality "increases of less than one per cent" (~0.3%) on a time span of 80 years, cautioning that this estimate was "speculative" since at this time only a few cancer deaths are linked to the Chernobyl disaster.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernobyl_Forum_report_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernobyl_Forum_report-136">[134]</a></sup> The report says it is impossible to reliably predict the number of fatal cancers arising from the incident as small differences in assumptions can result in large differences in the estimated health costs. The report says it represents the consensus view of the eight UN organisations.</p> 
     <p>Of all 66,000 Belarusian emergency workers, by the mid-1990s only 150 (roughly 0.2%) were reported by their government as having died. In contrast, 5,722 casualties were reported among Ukrainian clean-up workers up to the year 1995, by the National Committee for Radiation Protection of the Ukrainian Population.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesDecade_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesDecade-91">[89]</a></sup></p>
