     <p>The four most harmful radionuclides spread from Chernobyl were <a href="/wiki/Iodine-131" title="Iodine-131">iodine-131</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caesium-134" title="Caesium-134" class="mw-redirect">caesium-134</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caesium-137" title="Caesium-137">caesium-137</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strontium-90" title="Strontium-90">strontium-90</a>, with half-lives of 8.02 days, 2.07 years, 30.2 years and 28.8 years respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-TORCH_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TORCH-137">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:8</sup> The iodine was initially viewed with less alarm than the other isotopes, because of its short half-life, but it is highly volatile, and now appears to have traveled furthest and caused the most severe health problems in the short term.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesDecade_91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesDecade-91">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:24</sup> Strontium, on the other hand, is the least volatile of the four, and of main concern in the areas near Chernobyl itself.<sup id="cite_ref-TORCH_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TORCH-137">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:8</sup> Iodine tends to become concentrated in thyroid and milk glands, leading, among other things, to increased incidence of thyroid cancers. Caesium tends to accumulate in vital organs such as the heart,<sup id="cite_ref-KuchinskayaWeWill_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KuchinskayaWeWill-138">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:133</sup> while strontium accumulates in bones, and may thus be a risk to bone-marrow and <a href="/wiki/Lymphocyte" title="Lymphocyte">lymphocytes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TORCH_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TORCH-137">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:8</sup> Radiation is most damaging to cells that are actively dividing. In adult mammals cell division is slow, except in hair follicles, skin, bone marrow and the gastrointestinal tract, which is why vomiting and hair loss are common symptoms of acute radiation sickness.<sup id="cite_ref-MycioWormwood_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MycioWormwood-139">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:42</sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Difficulties_in_assessment">Difficulties in assessment</span></h3> 
     <p>Health in Belarus and Ukraine has shown disturbing trends following the Chernobyl disaster. In Belarus, incidence of congenital defects had risen by 40% within six years of the accident, to the point that it became the principal cause of infant mortality.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesBelarus_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesBelarus-140">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:52</sup> There was a substantial increase in digestive, circulatory, nervous, respiratory and endocrine diseases and cancers, correlated with areas of high radioactive contamination, and in one especially contaminated district of Belarus, 95% of children were in 2005 reported to have at least one chronic illness.<sup id="cite_ref-KuchinskayaWeWill_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KuchinskayaWeWill-138">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:129, 199</sup> The Ukrainian Ministry of Health estimated in 1993 that roughly 70% of its population were unwell, with large increases in respiratory, blood and nervous system diseases.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesDecade_91-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesDecade-91">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:27</sup> By the year 2000, the number of Ukrainians claiming to be radiation 'sufferers' (<i>poterpili</i>) and receiving state benefits had jumped to 3.5 million, or 5% of the population. Many of these are populations resettled from contaminated zones, or former or current Chernobyl plant workers.<sup id="cite_ref-PetrynaLifeExposed_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetrynaLifeExposed-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:4–5</sup> According to <a href="/wiki/IAEA" title="IAEA" class="mw-redirect">IAEA</a>-affiliated scientific bodies, these apparent increases of ill health result partly from economic strains on these countries and poor health-care and nutrition; also, they suggest that increased medical vigilance following the accident has meant that many cases that would previously have gone unnoticed (especially of cancer) are now being registered.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesDecade_91-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesDecade-91">[89]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Of the approximately 600,000 'liquidators' that were engaged in the Chernobyl clean-up, roughly 50,000 were required to work as 'bio-robots', in conditions of such extreme radiation that electronic robots ceased to operate. These bio-robots are well-known figures within every village, housing block and work-collective. Most are prematurely aged and many have died, and leukaemia rates among them are substantially higher than in the wider population.<sup id="cite_ref-PetrynaLifeExposed_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetrynaLifeExposed-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:9–10,31</sup> According to ethnographer Adriana Petryna, birth defects appear to have increased in Ukraine as well. She describes gross deformities in the Kyev city hospital's neonatal unit, including one infant born to a Chernobyl worker, who had an extra finger, a deformed ear, his trachea missing and his gut external to his body. Hospital staff were on the whole cooperative, but warned Petryna that she would be forbidden to access any statistics; she could therefore only treat these cases as anecdotal evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-PetrynaLifeExposed_83-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetrynaLifeExposed-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:7–8</sup> Poor or inaccessible statistics has meant that causal connections are very difficult to make in both Belarus and Ukraine. It has been observed that Belarus in particular actively suppresses or ignores health-related research,<sup id="cite_ref-PetrynaLifeExposed_83-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetrynaLifeExposed-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:4–5</sup> a false economy estimated to cost the country ten times more than it saves.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesBelarus_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesBelarus-140">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:51–2</sup> One Belarusian villager describes: "We had a year once when almost every day there was a funeral. We must have buried about fifty people that year. Is it related to radiation? Who knows."<sup id="cite_ref-KuchinskayaWeWill_138-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KuchinskayaWeWill-138">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:259</sup></p> 
     <p>Under Soviet rule, the extent of radiation injury was systematically covered up. Most cases of <a href="/wiki/Acute_radiation_sickness" title="Acute radiation sickness" class="mw-redirect">acute radiation sickness</a> (ARS) were disguised as ‘Vegetovascular dystonia’ (VvD), a Soviet classification for a type of panic disorder with possible symptoms including heart palpitations, sweating, tremors, nausea, hypotension or hypertension, neurosis, spasms and seizures: symptoms which resemble the neurological effects of ARS. Declassified documents show that the Soviet Health Ministry ordered the systematic misdiagnosis of ARS as VvD, for all people who did not show gross signs of radiation sickness such as burns or hair loss, and for all 'liquidators' who had exceeded their maximum allowable dose. It appears that up to 17,500 people were intentionally misdiagnosed in this manner.<sup id="cite_ref-PetrynaLifeExposed_83-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetrynaLifeExposed-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:43–4</sup> Subsequent claims for health welfare were denied on the basis of this diagnosis or the application of other psychosocial medical categories (individual poor constitution; psychological self-induction).<sup id="cite_ref-PetrynaLifeExposed_83-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetrynaLifeExposed-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:11</sup> A key tool for Soviet denial was the '35 rem concept', whereby it was held that 35 rems was a safe radiation exposure for a lifetime, "based on international standards", and since most people near Chernobyl received less than that, their health complaints could be attributed to "radiophobia".<sup id="cite_ref-KuchinskayaWeWill_138-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KuchinskayaWeWill-138">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:47</sup></p> 
     <p>Both Belarus and Ukraine rely heavily on foreign aid and have been pressured to comply with international views of the disaster. For instance, in 2002 the World Bank advised Belarus to "shift its attention from calculating the impact of the accident to developing forward-looking activities directed at economic development and improvement in the quality of life of the affected people". Health-related government welfare was blamed for creating "the sense of victimization and dependency" and thus exacerbating psychosomatic disorders.<sup id="cite_ref-KuchinskayaWeWill_138-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KuchinskayaWeWill-138">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:98,101</sup> Belarus in particular has complied by ignoring or suppressing scientific research.<sup id="cite_ref-PetrynaLifeExposed_83-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetrynaLifeExposed-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:4–5</sup> Historian David Marples attributes this more to that government's weakness and apathy than a simple desire to avoid health costs.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesBelarus_140-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesBelarus-140">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:51–2</sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thyroid_cancer">Thyroid cancer</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Belarus_radioactivity_and_thyroid_cancer.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Belarus_radioactivity_and_thyroid_cancer.png/300px-Belarus_radioactivity_and_thyroid_cancer.png" width="300" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Belarus_radioactivity_and_thyroid_cancer.png/450px-Belarus_radioactivity_and_thyroid_cancer.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Belarus_radioactivity_and_thyroid_cancer.png/600px-Belarus_radioactivity_and_thyroid_cancer.png 2x" data-file-width="889" data-file-height="717"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Belarus_radioactivity_and_thyroid_cancer.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Thyroid_cancer" title="Thyroid cancer">Thyroid cancer</a> incidence in children and adolescents from Belarus after the Chernobyl accident
        <br> Yellow: Adults (19–34)
        <br> Blue: Adolescents (15–18)
        <br> Red: Children (0–14)
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The 2005 Chernobyl Forum report revealed thyroid cancer among children to be one of the main health impacts from the Chernobyl accident. In that publication more than 4000 cases were reported, and that there was no evidence of an increase in solid cancers or leukemia. It said that there was an increase in psychological problems among the affected population.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernobyl_Forum_report_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernobyl_Forum_report-136">[134]</a></sup> Dr Michael Repacholi, manager of WHO's Radiation Program reported that the 4000 cases of thyroid cancer resulted in nine deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>According to UNSCEAR, up to the year 2005, an excess of over 6000 cases of thyroid cancer have been reported. That is, over the estimated pre-accident baseline thyroid cancer rate, more than 6000 <a href="/wiki/Casual" title="Casual">casual</a> cases of thyroid cancer have been reported in children and adolescents exposed at the time of the accident, a number that is expected to increase. They concluded that there is no other evidence of major health impacts from the radiation exposure.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernobyl_health_effects_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernobyl_health_effects-142">[140]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Well-differentiated <a href="/wiki/Thyroid_cancer" title="Thyroid cancer">thyroid cancers</a> are generally treatable,<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup> and when treated the <a href="/wiki/List_of_cancer_mortality_rates_in_the_United_States" title="List of cancer mortality rates in the United States">five-year survival rate of thyroid cancer</a> is 96%, and 92% after 30 years.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup> UNSCEAR had reported 15 deaths from thyroid cancer in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">International Atomic Energy Agency</a> (IAEA) also states that there has been no increase in the rate of <a href="/wiki/Birth_defect" title="Birth defect" class="mw-redirect">birth defects</a> or abnormalities, or <a href="/wiki/Solid_cancer" title="Solid cancer" class="mw-redirect">solid cancers</a> (such as lung cancer) corroborating UNSCEAR's assessments.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernobyl.27s_Legacy_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernobyl.27s_Legacy-146">[144]</a></sup> UNSCEAR does raise the possibility of long term genetic defects, pointing to a doubling of radiation-induced minisatellite <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutations</a> among children born in 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup> However, the risk of thyroid cancer associated with the Chernobyl accident is still high according to published studies.<sup id="cite_ref-Bogdanova_T_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanova_T-148">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dinets_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dinets-149">[147]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The German affiliate of the <a href="/wiki/International_Physicians_for_the_Prevention_of_Nuclear_War" title="International Physicians for the Prevention of Nuclear War">International Physicians for the Prevention of Nuclear War</a> (IPPNW) argued that more than 10,000 people are today affected by thyroid cancer and 50,000 cases are expected in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[148]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_health_disorders">Other health disorders</span></h3> 
     <p>Fred Mettler, a radiation expert at the University of New Mexico, puts the number of worldwide cancer deaths outside the highly contaminated zone at "perhaps" 5000, for a total of 9000 Chernobyl-associated fatal cancers, saying "the number is small (representing a few percent) relative to the normal spontaneous risk of cancer, but the numbers are large in absolute terms".<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup> The same report outlined studies based in data found in the Russian Registry from 1991 to 1998 that suggested that "of 61,000 Russian workers exposed to an average dose of 107 mSv about 5% of all fatalities that occurred may have been due to radiation exposure."<sup id="cite_ref-Chernobyl_Forum_report_136-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernobyl_Forum_report-136">[134]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The report went into depth about the risks to <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental health</a> of exaggerated fears about the effects of radiation.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernobyl_Forum_report_136-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernobyl_Forum_report-136">[134]</a></sup> According to the IAEA the "designation of the affected population as "victims" rather than "survivors" has led them to perceive themselves as helpless, weak and lacking control over their future". The IAEA says that this may have led to behaviour that has caused further health effects.<sup id="cite_ref-IAEA-situation_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IAEA-situation-152">[150]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Fred Mettler commented that 20 years later: "The population remains largely unsure of what the effects of radiation actually are and retain a sense of foreboding. A number of adolescents and young adults who have been exposed to modest or small amounts of radiation feel that they are somehow fatally flawed and there is no downside to using illicit drugs or having unprotected sex. To reverse such attitudes and behaviors will likely take years although some youth groups have begun programs that have promise."<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup> In addition, disadvantaged children around Chernobyl suffer from health problems that are attributable not only to the Chernobyl accident, but also to the poor state of post-Soviet health systems.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernobyl.27s_Legacy_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernobyl.27s_Legacy-146">[144]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Scientific_Committee_on_the_Effects_of_Atomic_Radiation" title="United Nations Scientific Committee on the Effects of Atomic Radiation">United Nations Scientific Committee on the Effects of Atomic Radiation</a> (UNSCEAR), part of the Chernobyl Forum, have produced their own assessments of the radiation effects.<sup id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR-154">[152]</a></sup> UNSCEAR was set up as a collaboration between various United Nation bodies, including the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organisation" title="World Health Organisation" class="mw-redirect">World Health Organisation</a>, after the atomic bomb attacks on Hiroshima and Nagasaki, to assess the long-term effects of radiation on human health.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[153]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deaths_due_to_radiation_exposure">Deaths due to radiation exposure</span></h3> 
     <p>The number of potential deaths arising from the Chernobyl disaster is heavily debated. The <a href="/wiki/WHO" title="WHO" class="mw-redirect">WHO</a>'s prediction of 4000 future cancer deaths in surrounding countries<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup> is based on the <a href="/wiki/Linear_no-threshold_model" title="Linear no-threshold model">Linear no-threshold model</a> (LNT), which assumes that the damage inflicted by radiation at low doses is directly proportional to the <a href="/wiki/Effective_dose_(radiation)" title="Effective dose (radiation)">dose</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[155]</a></sup> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Radiation_epidemiologist&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Radiation epidemiologist (page does not exist)">Radiation epidemiologist</a> Roy Shore contends that estimating health effects in a population from the LNT model "is not wise because of the uncertainties".<sup id="cite_ref-Science_2011_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Science_2011-158">[156]</a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:VOA_Markosian_-_Chernobyl02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/VOA_Markosian_-_Chernobyl02.jpg/250px-VOA_Markosian_-_Chernobyl02.jpg" width="250" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/VOA_Markosian_-_Chernobyl02.jpg/375px-VOA_Markosian_-_Chernobyl02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/VOA_Markosian_-_Chernobyl02.jpg/500px-VOA_Markosian_-_Chernobyl02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="518"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:VOA_Markosian_-_Chernobyl02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Radiation warning sign in Pripyat
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>According to the Union of Concerned Scientists the number of excess cancer deaths worldwide (including all contaminated areas) is approximately 27,000 based on the same LNT.<sup id="cite_ref-Union_of_Concerned_Scientists_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Union_of_Concerned_Scientists-159">[157]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Another study critical of the Chernobyl Forum report was commissioned by Greenpeace, which asserted that the most recently published figures indicate that in Belarus, Russia and Ukraine the accident could have resulted in 10,000–200,000 additional deaths in the period between 1990 and 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-greenpeace2006_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenpeace2006-17">[17]</a></sup> The Scientific Secretary of the Chernobyl Forum criticized the report's reliance on non-<a href="/wiki/Peer_review" title="Peer review">peer reviewed</a> locally produced studies. Although most of the study's sources were from peer-reviewed journals, including many Western medical journals, the higher mortality estimates were from non-peer-reviewed sources,<sup id="cite_ref-greenpeace2006_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenpeace2006-17">[17]</a></sup> while Gregory Härtl (spokesman for the WHO) suggested that the conclusions were motivated by ideology.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[158]</a></sup></p> 
     <p><i>Chernobyl: Consequences of the Catastrophe for People and the Environment</i> is an English translation of the 2007 Russian publication <i>Chernobyl</i>. It was published in 2009 by the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Academy_of_Sciences" title="New York Academy of Sciences">New York Academy of Sciences</a> in their <i>Annals of the New York Academy of Sciences</i>. It presents an analysis of scientific literature and concludes that medical records between 1986, the year of the accident, and 2004 reflect 985,000 premature deaths as a result of the radioactivity released.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-161">[159]</a></sup> Though, it was impossible to precisely determine what dose the affected people received, knowing the fact that the received doses varied strongly from one individual to the other in the population above which the radioactive cloud travelled, and also knowing the fact that one cannot tell for sure if a cancer in an individual from the former USSR is produced by radiation from Chernobyl accident or by other social or behavioral factors, such as smoking or alcohol drinking.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[160]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The authors suggest that most of the deaths were in Russia, Belarus and Ukraine, though others occurred worldwide throughout the many countries that were struck by radioactive fallout from Chernobyl. The literature analysis draws on over 1000 published titles and over 5000 internet and printed publications discussing the consequences of the Chernobyl disaster. The authors contend that those publications and papers were written by leading Eastern European authorities and have largely been downplayed or ignored by the IAEA and UNSCEAR.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-161">[159]</a></sup> This estimate has however been criticized as exaggerated, lacking a proper scientific base.<sup id="cite_ref-balonov_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-balonov-21">[21]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abortion_requests">Abortion requests</span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Radiophobia" title="Radiophobia">Radiophobia</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Following the accident, <a href="/wiki/Journalist" title="Journalist">journalists</a> mistrusted many medical professionals (such as the spokesman from the UK <a href="/wiki/National_Radiological_Protection_Board" title="National Radiological Protection Board">National Radiological Protection Board</a>), and in turn encouraged the public to mistrust them.<sup id="cite_ref-kasperson160_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kasperson160-163">[161]</a></sup> Throughout the European continent, in nations where <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a> is legal, many requests for induced abortions, of otherwise normal pregnancies, were obtained out of fears of radiation from Chernobyl, including an excess number of abortions in Denmark in the months following the accident.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[162]</a></sup> In Greece, following the accident many <a href="/wiki/Obstetrician" title="Obstetrician" class="mw-redirect">obstetricians</a> were unable to resist requests from worried pregnant mothers over fears of radiation. Although it was determined that the <a href="/wiki/Effective_dose_(radiation)" title="Effective dose (radiation)">effective dose</a> to <a href="/wiki/Greek_people" title="Greek people" class="mw-redirect">Greeks</a> would not exceed 1&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Sievert" title="Sievert">mSv</a> (100&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Rem_(unit)" title="Rem (unit)" class="mw-redirect">mrem</a>), a dose much lower than that which could induce embryonic abnormalities or other non-<a href="/wiki/Stochastic" title="Stochastic">stochastic</a> effects, there was an observed 2500 excess of otherwise wanted pregnancies being terminated, probably out of fear in the mother of radiation risk.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[163]</a></sup> A "slightly" above the expected number of requested induced abortions occurred in Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[165]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_conditions">Other conditions</span></h2> 
     <p>According to <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kenneth_Mossman&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kenneth Mossman (page does not exist)">Kenneth Mossman</a>, a Professor of <a href="/wiki/Health_Physics" title="Health Physics" class="mw-redirect">Health Physics</a> and member of the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Nuclear_Regulatory_Commission" title="U.S. Nuclear Regulatory Commission" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Nuclear Regulatory Commission</a> advisory committee,<sup id="cite_ref-Mossman_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mossman-168">[166]</a></sup> the "LNT philosophy is overly conservative, and low-level radiation may be less dangerous than commonly believed".<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[167]</a></sup> Yoshihisa Matsumoto, a radiation biologist at the Tokyo Institute of Technology, cites laboratory experiments on animals to suggest there must be a threshold dose below which DNA repair mechanisms can completely repair any radiation damage.<sup id="cite_ref-Science_2011_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Science_2011-158">[156]</a></sup> Mossman suggests that the proponents of the current model believe that being conservative is justified due to the uncertainties surrounding low level doses and it is better to have a "prudent public health policy".<sup id="cite_ref-Mossman_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mossman-168">[166]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Another significant issue is establishing consistent data on which to base the analysis of the impact of the Chernobyl accident. Since 1991 large social and political changes have occurred within the affected regions and these changes have had significant impact on the administration of health care, on socio-economic stability, and the manner in which statistical data is collected.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[168]</a></sup> Ronald Chesser, a radiation biologist at <a href="/wiki/Texas_Tech_University" title="Texas Tech University">Texas Tech University</a>, says that "the subsequent Soviet collapse, scarce funding, imprecise dosimetry, and difficulties tracking people over the years have limited the number of studies and their reliability".<sup id="cite_ref-Science_2011_158-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Science_2011-158">[156]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_and_political_consequences">Economic and political consequences</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:269px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:%D0%A3%D0%BB%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B0_%D0%9A%D0%B8%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0,_%D0%A7%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%8B%D0%BB%D1%8C.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/%D0%A3%D0%BB%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B0_%D0%9A%D0%B8%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%2C_%D0%A7%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%8B%D0%BB%D1%8C.jpg/267px-%D0%A3%D0%BB%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B0_%D0%9A%D0%B8%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%2C_%D0%A7%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%8B%D0%BB%D1%8C.jpg" width="267" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/%D0%A3%D0%BB%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B0_%D0%9A%D0%B8%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%2C_%D0%A7%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%8B%D0%BB%D1%8C.jpg/401px-%D0%A3%D0%BB%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B0_%D0%9A%D0%B8%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%2C_%D0%A7%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%8B%D0%BB%D1%8C.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/%D0%A3%D0%BB%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B0_%D0%9A%D0%B8%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%2C_%D0%A7%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%8B%D0%BB%D1%8C.jpg/534px-%D0%A3%D0%BB%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B0_%D0%9A%D0%B8%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%2C_%D0%A7%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%8B%D0%BB%D1%8C.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="589"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:%D0%A3%D0%BB%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B0_%D0%9A%D0%B8%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0,_%D0%A7%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%8B%D0%BB%D1%8C.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Abandoned buildings in Chernobyl
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:269px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Dmitry_Medvedev_in_Ukraine_26_April_2011-4.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Dmitry_Medvedev_in_Ukraine_26_April_2011-4.jpeg/267px-Dmitry_Medvedev_in_Ukraine_26_April_2011-4.jpeg" width="267" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Dmitry_Medvedev_in_Ukraine_26_April_2011-4.jpeg/401px-Dmitry_Medvedev_in_Ukraine_26_April_2011-4.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Dmitry_Medvedev_in_Ukraine_26_April_2011-4.jpeg/534px-Dmitry_Medvedev_in_Ukraine_26_April_2011-4.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="434"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Dmitry_Medvedev_in_Ukraine_26_April_2011-4.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Russian President 
        <a href="/wiki/Dmitry_Medvedev" title="Dmitry Medvedev">Medvedev</a> and Ukrainian President 
        <a href="/wiki/Viktor_Yanukovych" title="Viktor Yanukovych">Yanukovych</a> lay wreathes at a memorial to victims of the Chernobyl disaster on 26 April 2011.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>It is difficult to establish the total economic cost of the disaster. According to <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a>, the Soviet Union spent 18 billion rubles (the equivalent of US$18 billion at that time) on containment and decontamination, virtually bankrupting itself.<sup id="cite_ref-GorbachevBoC_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GorbachevBoC-3">[3]</a></sup> In Belarus the total cost over 30 years is estimated at US$235&nbsp;billion (in 2005 dollars).<sup id="cite_ref-Chernobyl.27s_Legacy_146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernobyl.27s_Legacy-146">[144]</a></sup> On-going costs are well known; in their 2003–2005 report, <a href="/wiki/The_Chernobyl_Forum" title="The Chernobyl Forum" class="mw-redirect">The Chernobyl Forum</a> stated that between 5% and 7% of government spending in Ukraine is still related to Chernobyl, while in Belarus over $13&nbsp;billion is thought to have been spent between 1991 and 2003, with 22% of national budget having been Chernobyl-related in 1991, falling to 6% by 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernobyl.27s_Legacy_146-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernobyl.27s_Legacy-146">[144]</a></sup> Much of the current cost relates to the payment of Chernobyl-related social benefits to some 7&nbsp;million people across the 3 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernobyl.27s_Legacy_146-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernobyl.27s_Legacy-146">[144]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>A significant economic impact at the time was the removal of 784,320&nbsp;ha (1,938,100 acres) of agricultural land and 694,200&nbsp;ha (1,715,000 acres) of forest from production. While much of this has been returned to use, agricultural production costs have risen due to the need for special cultivation techniques, fertilizers and additives.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernobyl.27s_Legacy_146-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernobyl.27s_Legacy-146">[144]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Politically, the accident gave great significance to the new Soviet policy of glasnost,<sup id="cite_ref-ShlyGlasnost_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ShlyGlasnost-171">[169]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PetrynaSarc_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetrynaSarc-172">[170]</a></sup> and helped forge closer Soviet–US relations at the end of the Cold War, through bioscientific cooperation.<sup id="cite_ref-PetrynaLE_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetrynaLE-173">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:44–48</sup> The disaster also became a key factor in the Union's eventual <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">1991 dissolution</a>, and a major influence in shaping the new <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PetrynaLE_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetrynaLE-173">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:20–21</sup></p> 
     <p>Both Ukraine and Belarus, in their first months of independence, lowered legal radiation thresholds from the Soviet Union's previous, elevated thresholds (from 35 rems per lifetime under the USSR to 7 rems per lifetime in Ukraine and 0.1 rems per year in Belarus).<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesBelarus_140-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesBelarus-140">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:46–7,119–124</sup> This required an expansion of territories that were considered contaminated. In Ukraine, over 500,000 people have now been resettled, many of whom have become applicants for medical and other welfare. Ukraine also maintains the destroyed reactor, for which it employs a very large workforce in order to keep individual exposure times low. Many of these workers have since registered disabilities and enrolled for welfare. In Ukraine, the Chernobyl disaster was an icon of the nationalist movement, symbolic of all that was wrong with the Soviet Union, and welfare became a key platform for winning independence. Ukraine has since developed a massive and burdensome welfare system that has become increasingly corrupt and ineffective.<sup id="cite_ref-PetrynaLE_173-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetrynaLE-173">[171]</a></sup> It has presented its greatly increased welfare demands since 1991 as a demonstration of its own moral legitimacy, and as an argument for needing foreign aid.<sup id="cite_ref-PetrynaLE_173-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetrynaLE-173">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:24</sup> Belarus, on the other hand, was politically weak when it gained independence, and looked to Moscow for guidance; in many ways it has returned to the old Soviet policy of secrecy and denial.<sup id="cite_ref-MarplesBelarus_140-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarplesBelarus-140">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:46–7,119–124</sup><sup id="cite_ref-PetrynaLE_173-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetrynaLE-173">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:22–24</sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span></h2> 
     <p>Following the accident, questions arose about the future of the plant and its eventual fate. All work on the unfinished reactors 5 and 6 was halted three years later. However, the trouble at the Chernobyl plant did not end with the disaster in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">reactor</a> 4. The damaged reactor was sealed off and 200 cubic meters (260&nbsp;cu&nbsp;yd) of concrete was placed between the disaster site and the operational buildings.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The work was managed by <a href="/wiki/Grigoriy_Mihaylovich_Naginskiy" title="Grigoriy Mihaylovich Naginskiy">Grigoriy Mihaylovich Naginskiy</a>, the Deputy Chief Engineer of Installation and Construction Directorate – 90. The Ukrainian government continued to let the three remaining reactors operate because of an energy shortage in the country.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decommissioning">Decommissioning</span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_Nuclear_Power_Plant#Decommissioning" title="Chernobyl Nuclear Power Plant">Chernobyl Nuclear Power Plant § Decommissioning</a>
     </div> 
     <p>In 1991, a fire broke out in the turbine building of reactor 2;<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[172]</a></sup> the authorities subsequently declared the reactor damaged beyond repair and was taken offline. Reactor 1 was decommissioned in November 1996 as part of a deal between the Ukrainian government and international organizations such as the IAEA to end operations at the plant. On 15 December 2000, then-President <a href="/wiki/Leonid_Kuchma" title="Leonid Kuchma">Leonid Kuchma</a> personally turned off Reactor 3 in an official ceremony, shutting down the entire site.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[173]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radioactive_waste_management">Radioactive waste management</span></h3> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Containment_of_the_reactor">Containment of the reactor</span></h4> 
     <p>The Chernobyl reactor is now enclosed in a large concrete sarcophagus, which was built quickly to allow continuing operation of the other reactors at the plant.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[174]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>A <a href="/wiki/New_Safe_Confinement" title="New Safe Confinement" class="mw-redirect">New Safe Confinement</a> was to have been built by the end of 2005; however, it has suffered ongoing delays and as of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Chernobyl_disaster&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, when construction finally began, was expected to be completed in 2013. This was delayed again to 2016, the end of the 30-year lifespan of the sarcophagus. The structure is being built adjacent to the existing shelter and will be slid into place on rails. It is to be a metal arch 105 metres (344&nbsp;ft) high and spanning 257 metres (843&nbsp;ft), to cover both unit 4 and the hastily built 1986 structure. The <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_Shelter_Fund" title="Chernobyl Shelter Fund">Chernobyl Shelter Fund</a>, set up in 1997, has received <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">€</a>810 million from international donors and projects to cover this project and previous work. It and the Nuclear Safety Account, also applied to Chernobyl decommissioning, are managed by the <a href="/wiki/European_Bank_for_Reconstruction_and_Development" title="European Bank for Reconstruction and Development">European Bank for Reconstruction and Development</a> (EBRD).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>By 2002, roughly 15,000 Ukrainian workers were still working within the Zone of Exclusion, maintaining the plant and performing other containment- and research-related tasks, often in dangerous conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-PetrynaLE_173-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetrynaLE-173">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:2</sup> A handful of Ukrainian scientists work inside the sarcophagus, but outsiders are rarely granted access. In 2006 an <a href="/wiki/60_Minutes_(Australian_TV_program)" title="60 Minutes (Australian TV program)" class="mw-redirect">Australian <i>60 Minutes</i></a> team led by reporter Richard Carleton and producer Stephen Rice were allowed to enter the sarcophagus for 15 minutes and film inside the control room.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[175]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>On 12 February 2013 a 600&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup> (6,500&nbsp;sq&nbsp;ft) section of the roof of the turbine-building, adjacent to the sarcophagus, collapsed. At first it was assumed that the roof collapsed because of the weight of snow on it. However the amount of snow was not exceptional, and the report of a Ukrainian fact-finding panel concluded that the part collapse of the turbine-building was the result of sloppy repair work and aging of the structure. The report mentioned the possibility that the repaired part of the turbine-building added a larger strain on the total structure than expected, and the braces in the roof were damaged by corrosion and sloppy welding. Experts such as Valentin Kupny, former deputy director of the nuclear plant, did warn that the complex was on the verge of a collapse, leaving the building in an extremely dangerous condition. A proposed reinforcement in 2005 was cancelled by a superior official. After the 12 February incident, radioactivity levels were up to 19 becquerels per cubic meter of air: 12 times normal. The report assumed radioactive materials from inside the structure spread to the surroundings after the roof collapsed. All 225 workers employed by the Chernobyl complex and the French company that is building the new shelter were evacuated shortly after the collapse. According to the managers of the complex, radiation levels around the plant were at normal levels (between 5 and 6&nbsp;µSv/h) and should not affect workers' health. According to Kupny the situation was underestimated by the Chernobyl nuclear complex managers, and information was kept secret.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[176]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[177]</a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Radioactive_materials_and_waste_management">Radioactive materials and waste management</span></h4> 
     <p>As of 2006<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Chernobyl_disaster&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, some fuel remained in the reactors at units 1 through 3, most of it in each unit's <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel_pool" title="Spent fuel pool">spent fuel pool</a>, as well as some material in a small spent fuel interim storage facility pond (ISF-1).</p> 
     <p>In 1999 a contract was signed for construction of a <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste" title="Radioactive waste">radioactive waste</a> management facility to store 25,000 used fuel assemblies from units 1–3 and other operational wastes, as well as material from decommissioning units 1–3 (which will be the first RBMK units decommissioned anywhere). The contract included a processing facility able to cut the RBMK fuel assemblies and to put the material in canisters, which were to be filled with <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert gas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Welded" title="Welded" class="mw-redirect">welded</a> shut.</p> 
     <p>The canisters were to be transported to <a href="/wiki/Dry_cask_storage" title="Dry cask storage">dry storage vaults</a>, where the fuel containers would be enclosed for up to 100 years. This facility, treating 2500 fuel assemblies per year, would be the first of its kind for RBMK fuel. However, after a significant part of the storage structures had been built, technical deficiencies in the concept emerged, and the contract was terminated in 2007. The interim spent fuel storage facility (ISF-2) will now be completed by others by mid-2013.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>Another contract has been let for a liquid radioactive waste treatment plant, to handle some 35,000 cubic meters of low- and intermediate-level liquid wastes at the site. This will need to be solidified and eventually buried along with solid wastes on site.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>In January 2008, the Ukrainian government announced a 4-stage decommissioning plan that incorporates the above waste activities and progresses towards a cleared site .<sup id="cite_ref-WNA-Chernobyl_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WNA-Chernobyl-100">[98]</a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lava-like_fuel-containing_materials_.28FCMs.29">Lava-like fuel-containing materials (FCMs)</span></h4> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Corium_(nuclear_reactor)" title="Corium (nuclear reactor)">Corium (nuclear reactor)</a>
     </div> 
     <p>According to official estimates, about 95% of the fuel in Reactor 4 at the time of the accident (about 180 <a href="/wiki/Metric_ton" title="Metric ton" class="mw-redirect">metric tons</a>) remains inside the shelter, with a total radioactivity of nearly 18 million <a href="/wiki/Curie" title="Curie">curies</a> (670&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">PBq</a>). The radioactive material consists of core fragments, dust, and lava-like "fuel containing materials" (FCM)—also called "<a href="/wiki/Corium_(nuclear_reactor)" title="Corium (nuclear reactor)">corium</a>"—that flowed through the wrecked reactor building before hardening into a <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramic</a> form.</p> 
     <p>Three different lavas are present in the basement of the reactor building: black, brown, and a <a href="/wiki/Porous" title="Porous" class="mw-redirect">porous</a> ceramic. The lava materials are <a href="/wiki/Silicate_glass" title="Silicate glass" class="mw-redirect">silicate glasses</a> with <a href="/wiki/Inclusion_(mineral)" title="Inclusion (mineral)">inclusions</a> of other materials within them. The porous lava is brown lava that dropped into water and thus cooled rapidly.</p> 
     <p>It is unclear how long the ceramic form will retard the release of radioactivity. From 1997 to 2002 a series of published papers suggested that the self-irradiation of the lava would convert all 1,200&nbsp;metric tons into a submicrometer and mobile powder within a few weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[178]</a></sup> But it has been reported that the degradation of the lava is likely to be a slow and gradual process rather than sudden and rapid.<sup id="cite_ref-Borovoi2006_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borovoi2006-181">[179]</a></sup> The same paper states that the loss of <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> from the wrecked reactor is only 10&nbsp;kg (22&nbsp;lb) per year; this low rate of uranium leaching suggests that the lava is resisting its environment.<sup id="cite_ref-Borovoi2006_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borovoi2006-181">[179]</a></sup> The paper also states that when the shelter is improved, the leaching rate of the lava will decrease.<sup id="cite_ref-Borovoi2006_181-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borovoi2006-181">[179]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Some of the surfaces of the lava flows have started to show new uranium minerals such as <span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:85%;text-align:left"><br> 4</span>(UO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:85%;text-align:left"><br> 2</span>)(CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:85%;text-align:left"><br> 3</span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:85%;text-align:left"><br> 3</span></span> and <a href="/wiki/Uranyl_carbonate" title="Uranyl carbonate">uranyl carbonate</a>. However, the level of radioactivity is such that during 100 years, the lava's self irradiation (<span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7016200000000000000♠</span>2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em">×</span>10<sup>16</sup></span> α&nbsp;decays per gram and 2 to <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7005500000000000000♠</span>5<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em">×</span>10<sup>5</sup>&nbsp;Gy</span> of β or γ) will fall short of the level required to greatly change the properties of <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a> (10<sup>18</sup> α decays per gram and 10<sup>8</sup> to 10<sup>9</sup>&nbsp;Gy of β or γ). Also the lava's rate of dissolution in water is very low (10<sup>−7</sup>&nbsp;g·cm<sup>−2</sup>·day<sup>−1</sup>), suggesting that the lava is unlikely to dissolve in water.<sup id="cite_ref-Borovoi2006_181-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borovoi2006-181">[179]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Exclusion_Zone">The Exclusion Zone</span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_Exclusion_Zone" title="Chernobyl Exclusion Zone">Chernobyl Exclusion Zone</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Entrance_to_zone_of_alienation_around_Chernobyl.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Entrance_to_zone_of_alienation_around_Chernobyl.jpg/220px-Entrance_to_zone_of_alienation_around_Chernobyl.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Entrance_to_zone_of_alienation_around_Chernobyl.jpg/330px-Entrance_to_zone_of_alienation_around_Chernobyl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Entrance_to_zone_of_alienation_around_Chernobyl.jpg/440px-Entrance_to_zone_of_alienation_around_Chernobyl.jpg 2x" data-file-width="736" data-file-height="520"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Entrance_to_zone_of_alienation_around_Chernobyl.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Entrance to the 
        <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_Exclusion_Zone" title="Chernobyl Exclusion Zone">zone of alienation</a> around Chernobyl
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>An area originally extending 30 kilometres (19&nbsp;mi) in all directions from the plant is officially called the "zone of alienation". It is largely uninhabited, except for about 300 residents who have refused to leave. The area has largely reverted to forest, and has been overrun by wildlife because of a lack of competition with humans for space and resources. Even today, radiation levels are so high that the workers responsible for rebuilding the sarcophagus are only allowed to work five hours a day for one month before taking 15 days of rest. Ukrainian officials estimate the area will not be safe for human life again for another 20,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-TimeDisaster_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TimeDisaster-59">[57]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2011 Ukraine opened up the sealed zone around the Chernobyl reactor to tourists who wish to learn more about the tragedy that occurred in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[180]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[181]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forest_fires">Forest fires</span></h3> 
     <p>If the forests that have been contaminated by radioactive material catch on fire, they will spread the radioactive material further outwards in the smoke.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[182]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[183]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recovery_projects">Recovery projects</span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Chernobyl_Shelter_Fund">The Chernobyl Shelter Fund</span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles: 
      <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_Shelter_Fund" title="Chernobyl Shelter Fund">Chernobyl Shelter Fund</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_New_Safe_Confinement" title="Chernobyl New Safe Confinement">Chernobyl New Safe Confinement</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The Chernobyl Shelter Fund was established in 1997 at the Denver <a href="/wiki/23rd_G8_summit" title="23rd G8 summit">23rd G8 summit</a> to finance the Shelter Implementation Plan (SIP). The plan calls for transforming the site into an ecologically safe condition by means of stabilization of the sarcophagus followed by construction of a New Safe Confinement (NSC). While the original cost estimate for the SIP was US$768&nbsp;million, the 2006 estimate was $1.2&nbsp;billion. The SIP is being managed by a consortium of <a href="/wiki/Bechtel" title="Bechtel">Bechtel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battelle_Memorial_Institute" title="Battelle Memorial Institute">Battelle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/%C3%89lectricit%C3%A9_de_France" title="Électricité de France">Électricité de France</a>, and conceptual design for the NSC consists of a movable arch, constructed away from the shelter to avoid high radiation, to be slid over the sarcophagus. The NSC is expected to be completed in 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[184]</a></sup> and will be the largest movable structure ever built.</p> 
     <p>Dimensions:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>Span: 270&nbsp;m (886&nbsp;ft)</li> 
      <li>Height: 100&nbsp;m (330&nbsp;ft)</li> 
      <li>Length: 150&nbsp;m (492&nbsp;ft)</li> 
     </ul> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_United_Nations_Development_Programme">The United Nations Development Programme</span></h3> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Programme" title="United Nations Development Programme">United Nations Development Programme</a> has launched in 2003 a specific project called the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_Recovery_and_Development_Programme" title="Chernobyl Recovery and Development Programme">Chernobyl Recovery and Development Programme</a> (CRDP) for the recovery of the affected areas.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[185]</a></sup> The programme was initiated in February 2002 based on the recommendations in the report on Human Consequences of the Chernobyl Nuclear Accident. The main goal of the CRDP's activities is supporting the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ukraine" title="Government of Ukraine">Government of Ukraine</a> in mitigating long-term social, economic, and ecological consequences of the Chernobyl catastrophe. CRDP works in the four most Chernobyl-affected areas in Ukraine: <a href="/wiki/Kyivska" title="Kyivska" class="mw-redirect">Kyivska</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhytomyr_Oblast" title="Zhytomyr Oblast">Zhytomyrska</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chernihiv_Oblast" title="Chernihiv Oblast">Chernihivska</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rivnenska" title="Rivnenska" class="mw-redirect">Rivnenska</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_International_Project_on_the_Health_Effects_of_the_Chernobyl_Accident">The International Project on the Health Effects of the Chernobyl Accident</span></h3> 
     <p>The International Project on the Health Effects of the Chernobyl Accident (IPEHCA) was created and received <a href="/wiki/US_$" title="US $" class="mw-redirect">US $</a>20&nbsp;million, mainly from Japan, in hopes of discovering the main cause of health problems due to <sup>131</sup>I radiation. These funds were divided between Ukraine, Belarus, and Russia, the three main affected countries, for further investigation of health effects. As there was significant corruption in former Soviet countries, most of the foreign aid was given to Russia, and no positive outcome from this money has been demonstrated.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chernobyl_Children_International">Chernobyl Children International</span></h3> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_Children_International" title="Chernobyl Children International">Chernobyl Children International</a> (CCI) is a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>-accredited, <a href="/wiki/Non-profit" title="Non-profit" class="mw-redirect">non-profit</a>, international development, medical, and humanitarian organisation that works with children, families and communities that continue to be affected by the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_accident" title="Chernobyl accident" class="mw-redirect">Chernobyl nuclear disaster</a>. The organisation's founder and chief executive is <a href="/wiki/Adi_Roche" title="Adi Roche">Adi Roche</a>, the Irish humanitarian and peace campaigner. The CCI was founded in 1991 in response to an appeal from <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukrainian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarusian</a> doctors for aid. Roche then began organising 'rest and recuperation' holidays for a few Chernobyl children. Recruiting Irish families who would welcome and care for them, CCI expanded into the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcroche_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcroche-188">[186]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wcahon_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wcahon-189">[187]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Over its lifetime, the organisation has grown in strength and numbers. It works closely with the Belarusian government, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, and many thousands of volunteers worldwide to deliver a broad range of supports to the children and the wider community. It also acts as an advocate for the rights of those affected by the Chernobyl explosion, and engages in research and outreach activities to encourage the rest of the world to remember the victims and understand the long-term impact on their lives.<sup id="cite_ref-wcahon_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wcahon-189">[187]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbcroche_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcroche-188">[186]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemoration">Commemoration</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A9dailles_liquidateurs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/M%C3%A9dailles_liquidateurs.jpg/220px-M%C3%A9dailles_liquidateurs.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/M%C3%A9dailles_liquidateurs.jpg/330px-M%C3%A9dailles_liquidateurs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/M%C3%A9dailles_liquidateurs.jpg/440px-M%C3%A9dailles_liquidateurs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="1050"></a>

       </div>

     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright">

       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">

        </div> 200,000 
        <a href="/wiki/Karbovanets" title="Karbovanets" class="mw-redirect">karbovanets</a> coin issued by the 
        <a href="/wiki/National_Bank_of_Ukraine" title="National Bank of Ukraine">National Bank of Ukraine</a> to commemorate the 10th anniversary of the Chernobyl disaster
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p><i>The Front Veranda</i> (1986), a lithograph by <a href="/wiki/Susan_Dorothea_White" title="Susan Dorothea White">Susan Dorothea White</a> in the <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Australia" title="National Gallery of Australia">National Gallery of Australia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[188]</a></sup> exemplifies worldwide awareness of the event. <i>Heavy Water: A Film for Chernobyl</i> was released by <a href="/wiki/Seventh_Art_Productions" title="Seventh Art Productions" class="mw-redirect">Seventh Art</a> in 2006 to commemorate the disaster through poetry and first-hand accounts.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[189]</a></sup> The film secured the Best Short Documentary at <a href="/wiki/Cinequest_Film_Festival" title="Cinequest Film Festival">Cinequest Film Festival</a> as well as the Rhode Island "best score" award<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[190]</a></sup> along with a screening at Tate Modern.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[191]</a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_Way" title="Chernobyl Way">Chernobyl Way</a> is an annual rally run on 26 April by the opposition in Belarus as a remembrance of the Chernobyl disaster.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_impact">Cultural impact</span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles: 
      <a href="/wiki/Cultural_impact_of_the_Chernobyl_disaster" title="Cultural impact of the Chernobyl disaster">Cultural impact of the Chernobyl disaster</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_debate" title="Nuclear power debate">Nuclear power debate</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The Chernobyl accident attracted a great deal of interest. Because of the distrust that many people (both within and outside the <a href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR" class="mw-redirect">USSR</a>) had in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> authorities, a great deal of debate about the situation at the site occurred in the <a href="/wiki/First_World" title="First World">First World</a> during the early days of the event. Because of defective <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_(information_gathering)" title="Intelligence (information gathering)" class="mw-redirect">intelligence</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Satellite_imagery" title="Satellite imagery">photographs taken from space</a>, it was thought that unit number three had also suffered a dire accident.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>Journalists mistrusted many professionals (such as the spokesman from the UK <a href="/wiki/NRPB" title="NRPB" class="mw-redirect">NRPB</a>), and in turn encouraged the public to mistrust them.<sup id="cite_ref-kasperson160_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kasperson160-163">[161]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In Italy, the Chernobyl accident was reflected in the outcome of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_nuclear_power_referendum,_1987" title="Italian nuclear power referendum, 1987" class="mw-redirect">1987 referendum</a>. As a result of that referendum, Italy began phasing out its nuclear power plants in 1988, a decision that was effectively <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_Italy" title="Nuclear power in Italy">reversed in 2008</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Italian_referendums,_2011#Nuclear_power" title="Italian referendums, 2011">referendum in 2011</a> reiterated Italians' strong objections to nuclear power, thus abrogating the government's decision of 2008.</p> 
     <p>In Germany the Chernobyl accident led to the creation of a federal environment ministry, after several states had already created such a post. The minister was given the authority over reactor safety as well, which the current minister still holds as of 2015. The events are also credited with strengthening the anti-nuclear power movement, which culminated in the decision to end the use of nuclear power that was made by the 1998–2005 Schröder government.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2> 
     <div class="div-col columns column-count column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;"> 
      <ul>

       <li><a href="/wiki/Children_of_Chernobyl_Benefit_Concert" title="Children of Chernobyl Benefit Concert">Children of Chernobyl Benefit Concert</a></li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Convention_on_Early_Notification_of_a_Nuclear_Accident" title="Convention on Early Notification of a Nuclear Accident">Convention on Early Notification of a Nuclear Accident</a></li>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References_and_further_reading">References and further reading</span></h2> 
     <p><b>Explanatory notes</b></p> 
     <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;"> 
      <ol class="references"> 
       <li id="cite_note-Kagarlitsky-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Kagarlitsky_24-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"No one believed the first newspaper reports, which patently understated the scale of the catastrophe and often contradicted one another. The confidence of readers was re-established only after the press was allowed to examine the events in detail without the original censorship restrictions. The policy of openness (<a href="/wiki/Glasnost" title="Glasnost">glasnost</a>) and 'uncompromising criticism' of outmoded arrangements had been proclaimed back at the 27th Congress (of <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Soviet_Union" title="Communist Party of Soviet Union" class="mw-redirect">KPSS</a>), but it was only in the tragic days following the Chernobyl disaster that glasnost began to change from an official slogan into an everyday practice. The truth about Chernobyl that eventually hit the newspapers opened the way to a more truthful examination of other social problems. More and more articles were written about drug abuse, crime, corruption and the mistakes of leaders of various ranks. A wave of 'bad news' swept over the readers in 1986–87, shaking the consciousness of society. Many were horrified to find out about the numerous calamities of which they had previously had no idea. It often seemed to people that there were many more outrages in the epoch of <a href="/wiki/Perestroika" title="Perestroika">perestroika</a> than before although, in fact, they had simply not been informed about them previously." -Kagarlitsky pp. 333–334</span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-Medvedev_Z-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Medvedev_Z_31-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The mere fact that the operators were carrying out an experiment that had not been approved by higher officials indicates that something was wrong with the chain of command. The State Committee on Safety in the Atomic Power Industry is permanently represented at the Chernobyl station. Yet the engineers and experts in that office were not informed about the program. In part, the tragedy was the product of administrative anarchy or the attempt to keep everything secret." Medvedev, Z., pp. 18–20</span></li> 
      </ol> 
     </div>

       <li id="cite_note-nf-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-nf_36-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://nuclearfissionary.com/2010/03/03/what-happened-at-chernobyl/">"What Happened at Chernobyl?"</a>. Nuclear Fissionary<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">12 January</span> 2011</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AChernobyl+disaster&amp;rft.btitle=What+Happened+at+Chernobyl%3F&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fnuclearfissionary.com%2F2010%2F03%2F03%2Fwhat-happened-at-chernobyl%2F&amp;rft.pub=Nuclear+Fissionary&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&nbsp;</span></span></span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The accumulation of Xe-135 in the core is burned out by neutrons. Higher power settings bring higher <a href="/wiki/Neutron_flux" title="Neutron flux">neutron flux</a> and burn the xenon out more quickly. Conversely, low power settings result in the accumulation of xenon.</span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://accidont.ru/expert.html">The information on accident at the Chernobyl NPP and its consequences, prepared for IAEA</a>, Atomic Energy, v. 61, 1986, pp. 308–320.</span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The RBMK is a boiling water reactor, so in-core boiling is normal at higher power levels. The RBMK design has a negative void coefficient above 700&nbsp;MW.</span></li>

       <li id="cite_note-autogenerated1-161"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-autogenerated1_161-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-autogenerated1_161-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.nyas.org/publications/annals/Detail.aspx?cid=f3f3bd16-51ba-4d7b-a086-753f44b3bfc1">"Details"</a>. <i>Annals of the New York Academy of Sciences</i>. Annals of the New York Academy of Sciences<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">15 March</span> 2011</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AChernobyl+disaster&amp;rft.atitle=Details&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.nyas.org%2Fpublications%2Fannals%2FDetail.aspx%3Fcid%3Df3f3bd16-51ba-4d7b-a086-753f44b3bfc1&amp;rft.jtitle=Annals+of+the+New+York+Academy+of+Sciences&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&nbsp;</span></span></span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-162"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-162">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">- pp. 85-86, pp. 92-93 in "Radiation: What It Is, What You Need To Know" by Robert Peter Gale, M.D., Ph.D. and Eric Lax. Publisher: Alfred A. Knopf, New York, 2013. ("The correct number of Chernobyl-related cancers will never be known, in part because of the considerable uncertainties in estimating cancers and cancer deaths. Especially problematic is the controversy about whether very low doses of radiation, especially if given over a prolonged interval, increase cancer risk. There are other difficulties as well. For one, we do not know precisely what radiation dose most people received. People who were indoors when the radioactive plume passed received much less radiation than those who were outdoors. However, because most people did not know when the radioactive plume passed, they cannot accurately reconstruct their whereabouts at that time. Also, many people were evacuated from contaminated land at different times and thus received very different doses from ground and food contamination. Next we have the geopolitical reality that many of the exposed people no longer live in the Chernobyl area. Living elsewhere, even in other countries, they are <a href="/wiki/Lost_to_follow-up" title="Lost to follow-up">lost to follow-up</a>. The Chernobyl accident was relatively quickly followed by the dissolution of the Soviet Union, whereupon many people's lifestyles but perhaps not their lives changed, mostly for the worse. For example, cigarette smoking and alcohol consumption increased, resulting in a profound drop in life expectancy. Both activities are correlated with increased cancer risk independent of radiation exposure. Sorting out any changes in cancer incidence or prevalence will be difficult at best. […] Fourth, high-quality cancer registries were absent before and even after the accident, making it impossible to know with certainty the background rate of most cancers before the accident.")</span></li>

(1991). <i>Communicating Risks to the Public: International Perspectives</i>. Berlin: Springer Science and Media. pp.&nbsp;160–162. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-7923-0601-5" title="Special:BookSources/0-7923-0601-5">0-7923-0601-5</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AChernobyl+disaster&amp;rft.aufirst=Roger+E.&amp;rft.aulast=Kasperson&amp;rft.au=Stallen%2C+Pieter+Jan+M.&amp;rft.btitle=Communicating+Risks+to+the+Public%3A+International+Perspectives&amp;rft.date=1991&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-7923-0601-5&amp;rft.pages=160-162&amp;rft.place=Berlin&amp;rft.pub=Springer+Science+and+Media&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&nbsp;</span></span></span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-164"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-164">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Knudsen, L. B. (1991). Random House UK (paperback 1997). <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-7493-1633-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-7493-1633-4">978-0-7493-1633-4</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AChernobyl+disaster&amp;rft.aufirst=Piers+Paul&amp;rft.aulast=Read&amp;rft.btitle=Ablaze%21+The+Story+of+the+Heroes+and+Victims+of+Chernobyl&amp;rft.date=1993&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-7493-1633-4&amp;rft.pub=Random+House+UK+%28paperback+1997%29&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&nbsp;</span></span></li> 
       <li><cite class="citation book">Shcherbak, Yurii (1991). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.x-libri.ru/elib/sherb000/index.htm"><i>Chernobyl</i></a> (in Russian and English). New York: Soviet Writers/St. Martin's Press. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-312-03097-5" title="Special:BookSources/0-312-03097-5">0-312-03097-5</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AChernobyl+disaster&amp;rft.aufirst=Yurii&amp;rft.aulast=Shcherbak&amp;rft.btitle=Chernobyl&amp;rft.date=1991&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.x-libri.ru%2Felib%2Fsherb000%2Findex.htm&amp;rft.isbn=0-312-03097-5&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Soviet+Writers%2FSt.+Martin%27s+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&nbsp;</span></span></li> 
       <li>Yaroshinskaya, Alla A. <i>Chernobyl: Crime Without Punishment.</i> Piscataway, NJ: Transaction Publisher, 2015.</li> 
       <li>Gerd Ludwig and Lois Lammerhuber: <i>Der lange Schatten von Tschernobyl - The Long Shadow of Chernobyl - L'ombre de Tchernobyl</i>. Edition Lammerhuber, 2014, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9783901753664" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-3901753664</a>. Illustrated book containing photos made 2013 within the reactor hull. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.nationalgeographic.de/aktuelles/gerd-ludwig-tschernobyl-ist-das-zentrum-meiner-arbeit">Interview with the photographer</a> (in German)</li> 
       <li>Vjačeslav Šestopalov,et al.: <i>Groundwater vulnerability: Chernobyl nuclear disaster.</i> American Geophysical Union, Washington 2015, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781118962190" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-1-118-96219-0</a>.</li> 
      </ul>
